	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">History of physics</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newtons_cradle_animation_book_2.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Newtons_cradle_animation_book_2.gif/220px-Newtons_cradle_animation_book_2.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Newtons_cradle_animation_book_2.gif/330px-Newtons_cradle_animation_book_2.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Newtons_cradle_animation_book_2.gif/440px-Newtons_cradle_animation_book_2.gif 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newtons_cradle_animation_book_2.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"If I have seen further it is by standing on the shoulders of Giants." &#8211;&#160;<a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>&#8201;<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">Physics</a> is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> whose primary objects of study are <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>.  Discoveries of physics find applications throughout the <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural sciences</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a>, since matter and energy are the basic constituents of the natural world. Some other domains of study—more limited in their scope—may be considered branches that have split off from physics to become sciences in their own right. Physics today may be divided loosely into <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">classical physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_physics" title="Modern physics">modern physics</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_history">Ancient history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Ancient history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_astronomy" title="History of astronomy">History of astronomy</a></div>
<p>Elements of what became physics were drawn primarily from the fields of <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mechanics" title="Mechanics">mechanics</a>, which were methodologically united through the study of <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a>. These mathematical disciplines began in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">antiquity</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonians</a> and with <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic</a> writers such as <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ancient_philosophy" title="Ancient philosophy">Ancient philosophy</a>, meanwhile&#160;– including what was called "<a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)">physics</a>"&#160;– focused on explaining nature through ideas such as <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Aristotle#Causality,_the_four_causes" title="Aristotle">four types of "cause"</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Greece">Ancient Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ancient Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The move towards a rational understanding of nature began at least since the <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">Archaic period</a> in Greece (650–480 <a href="/wiki/BCE" class="mw-redirect" title="BCE">BCE</a>) with the <a href="/wiki/Presocratics" class="mw-redirect" title="Presocratics">Pre-Socratic philosophers</a>.  The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Thales_of_Miletus" title="Thales of Miletus">Thales of Miletus</a> (7th and 6th centuries BCE), dubbed "the Father of Science" for refusing to accept various supernatural, religious or mythological explanations for natural <a href="/wiki/Phenomenon" title="Phenomenon">phenomena</a>, proclaimed that every event had a natural cause.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Thales also made advancements in 580 BCE by suggesting that water is <a href="/wiki/Arche" title="Arche">the basic element</a>, experimenting with the attraction between <a href="/wiki/Magnet" title="Magnet">magnets</a> and rubbed <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a> and formulating the first recorded <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmologies</a>. <a href="/wiki/Anaximander" title="Anaximander">Anaximander</a>, famous for his proto-<a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> theory, disputed the Thales' ideas and proposed that rather than water, a substance called <i><a href="/wiki/Apeiron_(cosmology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Apeiron (cosmology)">apeiron</a></i> was the building block of all matter. Around 500 BCE, <a href="/wiki/Heraclitus" title="Heraclitus">Heraclitus</a> proposed that the only basic law governing the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a> was the principle of change and that nothing remains in the same state indefinitely. This observation made him one of the first scholars in ancient physics to address the role of <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a> in the universe, a key and sometimes contentious concept in modern and present-day physics.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The early physicist <a href="/wiki/Leucippus" title="Leucippus">Leucippus</a> (<a href="/wiki/Floruit" title="Floruit">fl.</a> first half of the 5th century BCE) adamantly opposed the idea of direct <a href="/wiki/Divine_providence" title="Divine providence">divine intervention</a> in the universe, proposing instead that natural phenomena had a natural cause. Leucippus and his student <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> were the first to develop the theory of <a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">atomism</a>, the idea that everything is composed entirely of various imperishable, indivisible elements called <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg/170px-Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg/255px-Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg/340px-Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1700" data-file-height="2275" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a><br />(384–322 <a href="/wiki/BCE" class="mw-redirect" title="BCE">BCE</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">classical period</a> in Greece (6th, 5th and 4th centuries BCE) and in <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic times</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a> slowly developed into an exciting and contentious field of study. <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">Ἀριστοτέλης</span>, <i>Aristotélēs</i>) (384 – 322 BCE), a student of <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, promoted the concept that observation of physical phenomena could ultimately lead to the discovery of the natural laws governing them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Aristotle's writings cover physics, <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theater" class="mw-redirect" title="Theater">theater</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">politics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forms_of_government" class="mw-redirect" title="Forms of government">government</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>. He wrote the first work which refers to that line of study as "Physics"&#160;– in the 4th century BCE, Aristotle founded the system known as <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_physics" title="Aristotelian physics">Aristotelian physics</a>. He attempted to explain ideas such as <a href="/wiki/Motion_(physics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Motion (physics)">motion</a> (and <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>) with the theory of <a href="/wiki/Classical_elements" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical elements">four elements</a>. Aristotle believed that all matter was made up of aether, or some combination of four elements: earth, water, air, and fire. According to Aristotle, these four terrestrial elements are capable of inter-transformation and move toward their natural place, so a stone falls downward toward the center of the cosmos, but flames rise upward toward the <a href="/wiki/Circumference" title="Circumference">circumference</a>. Eventually, <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_physics" title="Aristotelian physics">Aristotelian physics</a> became enormously popular for many centuries in Europe, informing the scientific and scholastic developments of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. It remained the mainstream scientific paradigm in Europe until the time of <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>.
</p><p>Early in Classical Greece, knowledge that the Earth is <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">spherical</a> ("round") was common. Around 240 BCE, as the result of <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes#Measurement_of_the_Earth&#39;s_circumference" title="Eratosthenes">a seminal experiment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> (276–194 BCE) accurately estimated its circumference. In contrast to Aristotle's geocentric views, <a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">Aristarchus of Samos</a> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">Ἀρίσταρχος</span>; c.310&#160;– c.230 BCE) presented an explicit argument for a <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentric model</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar system">Solar system</a>, i.e. for placing the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, not the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, at its centre. <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_of_Seleucia" title="Seleucus of Seleucia">Seleucus of Seleucia</a>, a follower of Aristarchus' heliocentric theory, stated that <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">the Earth rotated around its own axis</a>, which, in turn, <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">revolved around</a> the Sun. Though the arguments he used were lost, <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> stated that Seleucus was the first to prove the heliocentric system through reasoning.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg/220px-Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg/330px-Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg/440px-Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1364" data-file-height="1818" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The ancient Greek mathematician <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a>, famous for his ideas regarding <a href="/wiki/Fluid_mechanics" title="Fluid mechanics">fluid mechanics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buoyancy" title="Buoyancy">buoyancy</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In the 3rd century BCE, the <a href="/wiki/Greek_mathematics" title="Greek mathematics">Greek mathematician</a> <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes of Syracuse</a> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%88%CF%81%CF%87%CE%B9%CE%BC%CE%AE%CE%B4%CE%B7%CF%82" class="extiw" title="wikt:Ἀρχιμήδης">Ἀρχιμήδης</a></span> (287–212 BCE)&#160;– generally considered to be the greatest mathematician of antiquity and one of the greatest of all time&#160;– laid the foundations of <a href="/wiki/Fluid_statics" class="mw-redirect" title="Fluid statics">hydrostatics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Statics" title="Statics">statics</a> and calculated the underlying mathematics of the <a href="/wiki/Lever" title="Lever">lever</a>. A leading scientist of classical antiquity, Archimedes also developed elaborate systems of pulleys to move large objects with a minimum of effort. The <a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_screw" title="Archimedes&#39; screw">Archimedes' screw</a> underpins modern hydroengineering, and his machines of war helped to hold back the armies of Rome in the <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">First Punic War</a>. Archimedes even tore apart the arguments of Aristotle and his metaphysics, pointing out that it was impossible to separate mathematics and nature and proved it by converting mathematical theories into practical inventions. Furthermore, in his work <i><a href="/wiki/On_Floating_Bodies" title="On Floating Bodies">On Floating Bodies</a></i>, around 250 BCE, Archimedes developed the law of <a href="/wiki/Buoyancy" title="Buoyancy">buoyancy</a>, also known as <a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_principle" title="Archimedes&#39; principle">Archimedes' principle</a>. In mathematics, Archimedes used the method of exhaustion to calculate the area under the arc of a <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabola</a> with the summation of an infinite series, and gave a remarkably accurate approximation of <a href="/wiki/Pi" title="Pi">pi</a>. He also defined the <a href="/wiki/Archimedean_spiral" title="Archimedean spiral">spiral bearing his name</a>, formulae for the <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volumes</a> of surfaces of revolution and an ingenious system for expressing very large numbers. He also developed the principles of equilibrium states and <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass" title="Center of mass">centers of gravity</a>, ideas that would influence the well known scholars, Galileo, and Newton.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hipparchus" title="Hipparchus">Hipparchus</a> (190–120 BCE), focusing on astronomy and mathematics, used sophisticated geometrical techniques to map the motion of the stars and <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>, even predicting the times that <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">Solar eclipses</a> would happen. In addition, he added calculations of the distance of the Sun and Moon from the Earth, based upon his improvements to the observational instruments used at that time. Another of the most famous of the early physicists was <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> (90–168 CE), one of the leading minds during the time of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. Ptolemy was the author of several scientific treatises, at least three of which were of continuing importance to later Islamic and European science. The first is the astronomical treatise now known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a></i> (in Greek, Ἡ Μεγάλη Σύνταξις, "The Great Treatise", originally Μαθηματικὴ Σύνταξις, "Mathematical Treatise"). The second is the <i><a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)">Geography</a></i>, which is a thorough discussion of the geographic knowledge of the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman world</a>.
</p><p>Much of the accumulated knowledge of the ancient world was lost. Even of the works of the better known thinkers, few fragments survived. Although he wrote at least fourteen books, almost nothing of <a href="/wiki/Hipparchus" title="Hipparchus">Hipparchus</a>' direct work survived. Of the 150 reputed <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian</a> works, only 30 exist, and some of those are "little more than lecture notes"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (June 2018)">according to whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India_and_China">India and China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: India and China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_science_and_technology_in_China" title="History of science and technology in China">History of science and technology in China</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Indian_science_and_technology" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Indian science and technology">History of Indian science and technology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hindu-arabic1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Hindu-arabic1.jpg/220px-Hindu-arabic1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Hindu-arabic1.jpg/330px-Hindu-arabic1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Hindu-arabic1.jpg/440px-Hindu-arabic1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="549" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hindu-arabic1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Hindu-Arabic numeral system. The inscriptions on the <a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">edicts of Ashoka</a> (3rd century BCE) display this number system being used by the Imperial <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Mauryas</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Important physical and mathematical traditions also existed in <a href="/wiki/History_of_science_and_technology_in_China" title="History of science and technology in China">ancient Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Indian_science_and_technology" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Indian science and technology">Indian sciences</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG/260px-Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG" decoding="async" width="260" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG/390px-Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG/520px-Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG 2x" data-file-width="984" data-file-height="708" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Su_Song_Star_Map_1.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Star_map" class="mw-redirect" title="Star map">Star maps</a> by the 11th-century Chinese <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a> <a href="/wiki/Su_Song" title="Su Song">Su Song</a> are the oldest known <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">woodblock-printed</a> star maps to have survived to the present day. This example, dated 1092,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> employs <a href="/wiki/Cylindrical_projection" class="mw-redirect" title="Cylindrical projection">cylindrical projection</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Indian_philosophy" title="Indian philosophy">Indian philosophy</a>, Maharishi <a href="/wiki/Kanada_(philosopher)" title="Kanada (philosopher)">Kanada</a> was the first to systematically develop a theory of atomism around 200 BCE<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> though some authors have allotted him an earlier era in the 6th century BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> It was further elaborated by the <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_atomism" title="Buddhist atomism">Buddhist atomists</a> <a href="/wiki/Dharmakirti" title="Dharmakirti">Dharmakirti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dign%C4%81ga" title="Dignāga">Dignāga</a> during the 1st millennium CE.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pakudha_Kaccayana" title="Pakudha Kaccayana">Pakudha Kaccayana</a>, a 6th-century BCE Indian philosopher and contemporary of <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a>, had also propounded ideas about the atomic constitution of the material world. These philosophers believed that other elements (except ether) were physically palpable and hence comprised minuscule particles of matter. The last minuscule particle of matter that could not be subdivided further was termed <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">Parmanu</a>. These philosophers considered the atom to be indestructible and hence eternal. The Buddhists thought atoms to be minute objects unable to be seen to the naked eye that come into being and vanish in an instant. The <a href="/wiki/Vaisheshika" title="Vaisheshika">Vaisheshika</a> school of philosophers believed that an atom was a mere point in <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a>. It was also first to depict relations between motion and force applied. Indian theories about the atom are greatly abstract and enmeshed in philosophy as they were based on logic and not on personal experience or experimentation. In <a href="/wiki/Indian_astronomy" title="Indian astronomy">Indian astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Aryabhatiya" title="Aryabhatiya">Aryabhatiya</a></i> (499 CE) proposed the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth&#39;s rotation">Earth's rotation</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Nilakantha_Somayaji" title="Nilakantha Somayaji">Nilakantha Somayaji</a> (1444–1544) of the <a href="/wiki/Kerala_school_of_astronomy_and_mathematics" class="mw-redirect" title="Kerala school of astronomy and mathematics">Kerala school of astronomy and mathematics</a> proposed a semi-heliocentric model resembling the <a href="/wiki/Tychonic_system" title="Tychonic system">Tychonic system</a>.
</p><p>The study of <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetism</a> in <a href="/wiki/History_of_China#Ancient_China" title="History of China">Ancient China</a> dates back to the 4th century BCE. (in the <i>Book of the Devil Valley Master</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> A main contributor to this field was <a href="/wiki/Shen_Kuo" title="Shen Kuo">Shen Kuo</a> (1031–1095), a <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a> and statesman who was the first to describe the <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">magnetic-needle compass</a> used for navigation, as well as establishing the concept of <a href="/wiki/True_north" title="True north">true north</a>. In optics, Shen Kuo independently developed a <a href="/wiki/Camera_obscura" title="Camera obscura">camera obscura</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-needham_volume_4_part_1_98_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_4_part_1_98-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_world">Islamic world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Islamic world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Physics_in_medieval_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Physics in medieval Islam">Physics in medieval Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Science in the medieval Islamic world">Science in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Muslim_scientists" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Muslim scientists">List of Muslim scientists</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hazan.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Hazan.png/170px-Hazan.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f4/Hazan.png 1.5x" data-file-width="245" data-file-height="289" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hazan.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a> (c. 965–1040).</div></div></div>
<p>In the 7th to 15th centuries, scientific progress occurred in the Muslim world. Many classic works in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sassanian" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanian">Sassanian (Persian)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greek</a>, including the works of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, were translated into <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RobinsonCambridge_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RobinsonCambridge-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Important contributions were made by <a href="/wiki/Alhazen" class="mw-redirect" title="Alhazen">Ibn al-Haytham</a> (965–1040), an <a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">Arab</a> scientist, considered to be a founder of modern <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>. Ptolemy and Aristotle theorised that light either shone from the eye to illuminate objects or that "forms" emanated from objects themselves, whereas al-Haytham (known by the Latin name "Alhazen") suggested that light travels to the eye in rays from different points on an object. The works of Ibn al-Haytham and <a href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Rayh%C4%81n_B%C4%ABr%C5%ABn%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Abū Rayhān Bīrūnī">Abū Rayhān Bīrūnī</a> (973–1050), a Persian scientist, eventually passed on to Western Europe where they were studied by scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">Roger Bacon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Witelo" class="mw-redirect" title="Witelo">Witelo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ibn al-Haytham and Biruni were early proponents of the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>. Ibn al-Haytham is considered to be the "father of the modern scientific method" due to his emphasis on experimental data and <a href="/wiki/Reproducibility" title="Reproducibility">reproducibility</a> of its results.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The earliest methodical approach to <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a> in the modern sense is visible in the works of Ibn al-Haytham, who introduced an inductive-experimental method for achieving results.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Bīrūnī introduced early scientific methods for several different fields of <a href="/wiki/Inquiry" title="Inquiry">inquiry</a> during the 1020s and 1030s,<sup id="cite_ref-Sardar_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sardar-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> including an early experimental method for <a href="/wiki/Mechanics" title="Mechanics">mechanics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rozhanskaya-642_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rozhanskaya-642-16">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup> Biruni's methodology resembled the modern scientific method, particularly in his emphasis on repeated experimentation.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Ibn Sīnā</a> (980–1037), known as "Avicenna", was a polymath from <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a> (in present-day <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>) responsible for important contributions to physics, optics, philosophy and <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>. He published his theory of <a href="/wiki/Motion_(physics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Motion (physics)">motion</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Healing" class="mw-redirect" title="Book of Healing">Book of Healing</a></i> (1020), where he argued that an impetus is imparted to a projectile by the thrower, and believed that it was a temporary virtue that would decline even in a vacuum. He viewed it as persistent, requiring external forces such as <a href="/wiki/Air_resistance" class="mw-redirect" title="Air resistance">air resistance</a> to dissipate it.<sup id="cite_ref-Espinoza_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Espinoza-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nasr_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nasr-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sayili_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sayili-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Ibn Sina made a distinction between 'force' and 'inclination' (called "mayl"), and argued that an object gained mayl when the object is in opposition to its natural motion. He concluded that continuation of motion is attributed to the inclination that is transferred to the object, and that object will be in motion until the mayl is spent. He also claimed that projectile in a vacuum would not stop unless it is acted upon. This conception of motion is consistent with <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_first_law_of_motion" class="mw-redirect" title="Newton&#39;s first law of motion">Newton's first law of motion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">inertia</a>, which states that an object in motion will stay in motion unless it is acted on by an external force.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> This idea which dissented from the Aristotelian view was later described as "<a href="/wiki/Theory_of_impetus" title="Theory of impetus">impetus</a>" by <a href="/wiki/John_Buridan" class="mw-redirect" title="John Buridan">John Buridan</a>, who was influenced by Ibn Sina's <i>Book of Healing</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ibn_sina_and_buridan_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ibn_sina_and_buridan-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg/170px-Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/23/Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="240" data-file-height="380" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from <a href="/wiki/Mu%E1%B8%A5ammad_ibn_M%C5%ABs%C4%81_al-Khw%C4%81rizm%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī">al-Khwārizmī</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Compendious_Book_on_Calculation_by_Completion_and_Balancing" title="The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing">Algebra</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Omar_Khayy%C3%A1m" class="mw-redirect" title="Omar Khayyám">Omar Khayyám</a> (1048–1131), a Persian scientist, calculated the length of a solar year and was only out by a fraction of a second when compared to our modern day calculations. He used this to compose a calendar considered more accurate than the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a> that came along 500 years later.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> He is classified as one of the world's first great science communicators, said, for example to have convinced a <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi theologian</a> that the world turns on an axis.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hibat_Allah_Abu%27l-Barakat_al-Baghdaadi" class="mw-redirect" title="Hibat Allah Abu&#39;l-Barakat al-Baghdaadi">Hibat Allah Abu'l-Barakat al-Baghdaadi</a> (c. 1080-1165) adopted and modified Ibn Sina's theory on <a href="/wiki/Projectile_motion" title="Projectile motion">projectile motion</a>. In his <i>Kitab al-Mu'tabar</i>, Abu'l-Barakat stated that the mover imparts a violent inclination (<i>mayl qasri</i>) on the moved and that this diminishes as the moving object distances itself from the mover.<sup id="cite_ref-Gutman_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gutman-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> He also proposed an explanation of the <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a> of falling bodies by the accumulation of successive increments of <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">power</a> with successive increments of <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Shlomo_Pines" title="Shlomo Pines">Shlomo Pines</a>, al-Baghdaadi's theory of motion was "the oldest negation of Aristotle's fundamental dynamic law [namely, that a constant force produces a uniform motion], [and is thus an] anticipation in a vague fashion of the fundamental law of <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a> [namely, that a force applied continuously produces acceleration]."<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Jean Buridan and <a href="/wiki/Albert_of_Saxony_(philosopher)" title="Albert of Saxony (philosopher)">Albert of Saxony</a> later referred to Abu'l-Barakat in explaining that the acceleration of a falling body is a result of its increasing impetus.<sup id="cite_ref-Gutman_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gutman-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_Bajjah" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibn Bajjah">Ibn Bajjah</a> (c. 1085–1138), known as "Avempace" in Europe, proposed that for every force there is always a <a href="/wiki/Reaction_(physics)" title="Reaction (physics)">reaction</a> force. While he did not specify that these forces be equal, it was a precursor to <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_third_law_of_motion" class="mw-redirect" title="Newton&#39;s third law of motion">Newton's third law of motion</a> which states that for every action there is an equal and opposite reaction.<sup id="cite_ref-Franco_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Franco-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Ibn Bajjah was a critic of Ptolemy and he worked on creating a new theory of velocity to replace the one theorized by Aristotle. Two future philosophers supported the theories Avempace created, known as Avempacean dynamics. These philosophers were <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, a Catholic priest, and <a href="/wiki/Duns_Scotus" title="Duns Scotus">John Duns Scotus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo</a> went on to adopt Avempace's formula "that the velocity of a given object is the difference of the motive power of that object and the resistance of the medium of motion".<sup id="cite_ref-:1_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nasir_al-Din_al-Tusi" title="Nasir al-Din al-Tusi">Nasir al-Din al-Tusi</a> (1201–1274), a Persian astronomer and mathematician who died in Baghdad, authored the <i>Treasury of Astronomy</i>, a remarkably accurate table of planetary movements that reformed the existing planetary model of Roman astronomer Ptolemy by describing a uniform circular motion of all planets in their <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbits</a>. This work led to the later discovery, by one of his students, that planets actually have an elliptical orbit.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Copernicus later drew heavily on the work of al-Din al-Tusi and his students, but without acknowledgment.<sup id="cite_ref-cosmosmagazine.com_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cosmosmagazine.com-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The gradual chipping away of the Ptolemaic system paved the way for the revolutionary idea that the Earth actually orbited the Sun (<a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentrism</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_Europe">Medieval Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Medieval Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_impetus" title="Theory of impetus">Theory of impetus</a></div>
<p>Awareness of ancient works re-entered the West through <a href="/wiki/Latin_translations_of_the_12th_century" title="Latin translations of the 12th century">translations from Arabic to Latin</a>. Their re-introduction, combined with <a href="/wiki/Islam_and_Judaism" class="mw-redirect" title="Islam and Judaism">Judeo-Islamic</a> theological commentaries, had a great influence on <a href="/wiki/Medieval_philosophy" title="Medieval philosophy">Medieval philosophers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>. <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">Scholastic European scholars</a>, who sought to reconcile the philosophy of the ancient classical philosophers with <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">Christian theology</a>, proclaimed Aristotle the greatest thinker of the ancient world. In cases where they didn't directly contradict the Bible, Aristotelian physics became the foundation for the physical explanations of the European Churches.  Quantification became a core element of medieval physics.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Based on Aristotelian physics, Scholastic physics described things as moving according to their essential nature. Celestial objects were described as moving in circles, because perfect circular motion was considered an innate property of objects that existed in the uncorrupted realm of the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_spheres" title="Celestial spheres">celestial spheres</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_impetus" title="Theory of impetus">theory of impetus</a>, the ancestor to the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">inertia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a>, was developed along similar lines by <a href="/wiki/Medieval_philosophy" title="Medieval philosophy">medieval philosophers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/John_Philoponus" title="John Philoponus">John Philoponus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean_Buridan" title="Jean Buridan">Jean Buridan</a>. Motions below the lunar sphere were seen as imperfect, and thus could not be expected to exhibit consistent motion.  More idealized motion in the "sublunary" realm could only be achieved through <a href="/wiki/Mechanics_(Aristotle)" title="Mechanics (Aristotle)">artifice</a>, and prior to the 17th century, many did not view artificial experiments as a valid means of learning about the natural world.  Physical explanations in the sublunary realm revolved around tendencies.  Stones contained the element earth, and earthly objects tended to move in a straight line toward the centre of the earth (and the universe in the Aristotelian geocentric view) unless otherwise prevented from doing so.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_revolution">Scientific revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Scientific revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>During the 16th and 17th centuries, a large advancement of scientific progress known as the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific revolution">Scientific revolution</a> took place in Europe. Dissatisfaction with older philosophical approaches had begun earlier and had produced other changes in society, such as the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, but the revolution in science began when <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophers</a> began to mount a sustained attack on the <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">Scholastic</a> philosophical programme and supposed that mathematical descriptive schemes adopted from such fields as mechanics and astronomy could actually yield universally valid characterizations of motion and other concepts.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nicolaus_Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Nicolaus Copernicus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nikolaus_Kopernikus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Nikolaus_Kopernikus.jpg/200px-Nikolaus_Kopernikus.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Nikolaus_Kopernikus.jpg/300px-Nikolaus_Kopernikus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Nikolaus_Kopernikus.jpg/400px-Nikolaus_Kopernikus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1001" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nikolaus_Kopernikus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Polish astronomer <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a> (1473–1543) is remembered for his development of a <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentric</a> model of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar system">Solar system</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>A breakthrough in <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> was made by Polish astronomer <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a> (1473–1543) when, in 1543, he gave strong arguments for the <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentric model</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar system">Solar system</a>, ostensibly as a means to render tables charting planetary motion more accurate and to simplify their production. In heliocentric models of the Solar system, the Earth orbits the Sun along with other bodies in <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Earth's</a> <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a>, a contradiction according to the Greek-Egyptian astronomer Ptolemy (2nd century CE; see above), <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Ptolemaic system">whose system</a> placed the Earth at the center of the Universe and had been accepted for over 1,400 years. The Greek astronomer <a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">Aristarchus of Samos</a> (c.310&#160;– c.230 BCE) had suggested that the Earth revolves around the Sun, but Copernicus' reasoning led to lasting general acceptance of this "revolutionary" idea. Copernicus' book presenting the theory (<i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De revolutionibus orbium coelestium</a></i>, "On the Revolutions of the Celestial Spheres") was published just before his death in 1543 and, as it is now generally considered to mark the beginning of modern astronomy, is also considered to mark the beginning of the Scientific revolution.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Copernicus' new perspective, along with the accurate observations made by <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a>, enabled German astronomer <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> (1571–1630) to formulate <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler&#39;s laws of planetary motion">his laws regarding planetary motion</a> that remain in use today.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Galileo_Galilei">Galileo Galilei</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Galileo Galilei">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/170px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/255px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/340px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1180" data-file-height="1448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>, early proponent of the modern scientific worldview and method <br />(1564–1642)</div></div></div>
<p>The Italian mathematician, astronomer, and physicist <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> (1564–1642) was the central figure in the Scientific revolution and famous for his support for Copernicanism, his astronomical discoveries, empirical experiments and his improvement of the telescope. As a mathematician, Galileo's role in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_European_research_universities" class="mw-redirect" title="History of European research universities">university</a> culture of his era was subordinated to the three major topics of study: <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> (which was closely allied to philosophy).  Galileo, however, felt that the descriptive content of the technical disciplines warranted philosophical interest, particularly because mathematical analysis of astronomical observations&#160;– notably, Copernicus' analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Relative_motion" class="mw-redirect" title="Relative motion">relative motions</a> of the Sun, Earth, Moon, and planets&#160;– indicated that philosophers' statements about the nature of the universe could be shown to be in error.  Galileo also performed mechanical experiments, insisting that motion itself&#160;– regardless of whether it was produced "naturally" or "artificially" (i.e. deliberately)&#160;– had universally consistent characteristics that could be described mathematically.
</p><p>Galileo's early studies at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pisa" title="University of Pisa">University of Pisa</a> were in medicine, but he was soon drawn to mathematics and physics. At 19, he discovered (and, <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei#Career_as_a_scientist" title="Galileo Galilei">subsequently, verified</a>) the <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/isochronal" class="extiw" title="wikt:isochronal">isochronal</a> nature of the <a href="/wiki/Pendulum" title="Pendulum">pendulum</a> when, using his pulse, he timed the oscillations of a swinging lamp in <a href="/wiki/Piazza_dei_Miracoli" title="Piazza dei Miracoli">Pisa's cathedral</a> and found that it remained the same for each swing regardless of the swing's <a href="/wiki/Amplitude" title="Amplitude">amplitude</a>. He soon became known through his invention of a <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_balance" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrostatic balance">hydrostatic balance</a> and for his treatise on the <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass#Center_of_gravity" title="Center of mass">center of gravity</a> of solid bodies. While teaching at the University of Pisa (1589–92), he initiated his experiments concerning the laws of bodies in motion that brought results so contradictory to the accepted teachings of Aristotle that strong antagonism was aroused. He found that bodies do not fall with velocities <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(mathematics)" title="Proportionality (mathematics)">proportional</a> to their weights. The famous story in which Galileo is said to have <a href="/wiki/Galileo%27s_Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa_experiment" title="Galileo&#39;s Leaning Tower of Pisa experiment">dropped weights from</a> the <a href="/wiki/Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa" title="Leaning Tower of Pisa">Leaning Tower of Pisa</a> is apocryphal, but he did find that the <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_trajectory" class="mw-redirect" title="Ballistic trajectory">path of a projectile</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabola</a> and is credited with conclusions that anticipated <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton&#39;s laws of motion">Newton's laws of motion</a> (e.g. the notion of <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">inertia</a>). Among these is what is now called <a href="/wiki/Galilean_relativity" class="mw-redirect" title="Galilean relativity">Galilean relativity</a>, the first precisely formulated statement about properties of space and time outside <a href="/wiki/Three-dimensional_space" title="Three-dimensional space">three-dimensional geometry</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_Satellites.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_Satellites.jpg/220px-Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_Satellites.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="314" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_Satellites.jpg/330px-Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_Satellites.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_Satellites.jpg/440px-Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_Satellites.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1030" data-file-height="1470" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_Satellites.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A composite montage comparing <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> (lefthand side) and its four <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a> (top to bottom: <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>Galileo has been called the "father of modern <a href="/wiki/Observational_astronomy" title="Observational astronomy">observational astronomy</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> the "father of <a href="/wiki/Modern_physics" title="Modern physics">modern physics</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-Einstein_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> the "father of science",<sup id="cite_ref-Einstein_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and "the father of <a href="/wiki/Modern_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern science">modern science</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-finocchiaro2007_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finocchiaro2007-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a>, "Galileo, perhaps more than any other single person, was responsible for the birth of modern science."<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> As religious orthodoxy decreed a <a href="/wiki/Geocentricism" class="mw-redirect" title="Geocentricism">geocentric</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tychonic_system" title="Tychonic system">Tychonic</a> understanding of the Solar system, Galileo's support for <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentrism</a> provoked controversy and he was tried by the <a href="/wiki/Inquisition" title="Inquisition">Inquisition</a>. Found "vehemently suspect of heresy", he was forced to recant and spent the rest of his life under house arrest.
</p><p>The contributions that Galileo made to observational astronomy include the telescopic confirmation of the <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_Venus" title="Phases of Venus">phases of Venus</a>; his discovery, in 1609, of <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">Jupiter's four largest moons</a> (subsequently given the collective name of the "<a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a>"); and the observation and analysis of <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">sunspots</a>. Galileo also pursued applied science and technology, inventing, among other instruments, a military <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compass</a>. His discovery of the Jovian moons <a href="/wiki/Sidereus_Nuncius" title="Sidereus Nuncius">was published in 1610</a> and enabled him to obtain the position of mathematician and philosopher to the <a href="/wiki/Medici" class="mw-redirect" title="Medici">Medici</a> court.  As such, he was expected to engage in debates with philosophers in the Aristotelian tradition and received a large audience for his own publications such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Two_New_Sciences" title="Two New Sciences">Discourses and Mathematical Demonstrations Concerning Two New Sciences</a></i> (published abroad following his arrest for the publication of <i><a href="/wiki/Dialogue_Concerning_the_Two_Chief_World_Systems" title="Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems">Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</a></i>) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Assayer" title="The Assayer">The Assayer</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Galileo's interest in experimenting with and formulating mathematical descriptions of motion established experimentation as an integral part of natural philosophy.  This tradition, combining with the non-mathematical emphasis on the collection of "experimental histories" by philosophical reformists such as <a href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">William Gilbert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, drew a significant following in the years leading up to and following Galileo's death, including <a href="/wiki/Evangelista_Torricelli" title="Evangelista Torricelli">Evangelista Torricelli</a> and the participants in the <a href="/wiki/Accademia_del_Cimento" title="Accademia del Cimento">Accademia del Cimento</a> in Italy; <a href="/wiki/Marin_Mersenne" title="Marin Mersenne">Marin Mersenne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">Blaise Pascal</a> in France; <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a> in the Netherlands; and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> in England.
</p>
<h3><span id="Ren.C3.A9_Descartes"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="René_Descartes">René Descartes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: René Descartes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg/170px-Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg/255px-Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg/340px-Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="817" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a><br />(1596–1650)</div></div></div>
<p>The French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> (1596–1650) was well-connected to, and influential within, the experimental philosophy networks of the day. Descartes had a more ambitious agenda, however, which was geared toward replacing the Scholastic philosophical tradition altogether. Questioning the reality interpreted through the senses, Descartes sought to re-establish philosophical explanatory schemes by reducing all perceived phenomena to being attributable to the motion of an invisible sea of "corpuscles". (Notably, he reserved human thought and <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> from his scheme, holding these to be separate from the physical universe). In proposing this philosophical framework, Descartes supposed that different kinds of motion, such as that of planets versus that of terrestrial objects, were not fundamentally different, but were merely different manifestations of an endless chain of corpuscular motions obeying universal principles. Particularly influential were his explanations for circular astronomical motions in terms of the vortex motion of corpuscles in space (Descartes argued, in accord with the beliefs, if not the methods, of the Scholastics, that a <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a> could not exist), and his explanation of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> in terms of corpuscles pushing objects downward.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Descartes, like Galileo, was convinced of the importance of mathematical explanation, and he and his followers were key figures in the development of mathematics and geometry in the 17th century. Cartesian mathematical descriptions of motion held that all mathematical formulations had to be justifiable in terms of direct physical action, a position held by <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Huygens</a> and the German philosopher <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" class="mw-redirect" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Gottfried Leibniz</a>, who, while following in the Cartesian tradition, developed his own philosophical alternative to Scholasticism, which he outlined in his 1714 work, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Monadology" class="mw-redirect" title="The Monadology">The Monadology</a></i>. Descartes has been dubbed the 'Father of Modern Philosophy', and much subsequent <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western philosophy</a> is a response to his writings, which are studied closely to this day. In particular, his <i><a href="/wiki/Meditations_on_First_Philosophy" title="Meditations on First Philosophy">Meditations on First Philosophy</a></i> continues to be a standard text at most university philosophy departments. Descartes' influence in mathematics is equally apparent; the <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Cartesian coordinate system</a> — allowing algebraic equations to be expressed as geometric shapes in a two-dimensional coordinate system — was named after him. He is credited as the father of <a href="/wiki/Analytical_geometry" class="mw-redirect" title="Analytical geometry">analytical geometry</a>, the bridge between <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a>, important to the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_analysis" title="Mathematical analysis">analysis</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isaac_Newton">Isaac Newton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Isaac Newton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_classical_mechanics" title="History of classical mechanics">History of classical mechanics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/170px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/255px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/340px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg 2x" data-file-width="407" data-file-height="559" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sir <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a><br />(1642–1727)</div></div></div>
<p>The late 17th and early 18th centuries saw the achievements of the greatest figure of the Scientific revolution: <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Cambridge University">Cambridge University</a> physicist and mathematician <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Sir Isaac Newton</a> (1642-1727), considered by many to be the greatest and most influential scientist who ever lived. Newton, a fellow of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society of England</a>, combined his own discoveries in mechanics and astronomy to earlier ones to create a single system for describing the workings of the universe. Newton formulated <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton&#39;s laws of motion">three laws of motion</a> which formulated the relationship between motion and objects and also the <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton&#39;s law of universal gravitation">law of universal gravitation</a>, the latter of which could be used to explain the behavior not only of falling bodies on the earth but also planets and other celestial bodies. To arrive at his results, Newton invented one form of an entirely new branch of mathematics: <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a> (also invented independently by <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Gottfried Leibniz</a>), which was to become an essential tool in much of the later development in most branches of physics. Newton's findings were set forth in his <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica</a></i> ("Mathematical Principles of Natural Philosophy"), the publication of which in 1687 marked the beginning of the modern period of mechanics and astronomy.
</p><p>Newton was able to refute the Cartesian mechanical tradition that all motions should be explained with respect to the immediate force exerted by corpuscles. Using his three laws of motion and law of universal gravitation, Newton removed the idea that objects followed paths determined by natural shapes and instead demonstrated that not only regularly observed paths, but all the future motions of any body could be deduced mathematically based on knowledge of their existing motion, their <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">forces</a> acting upon them.  However, observed celestial motions did not precisely conform to a Newtonian treatment, and Newton, who was also deeply interested in <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>, imagined that God intervened to ensure the continued stability of the solar system.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz,_Bernhard_Christoph_Francke.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz%2C_Bernhard_Christoph_Francke.jpg/170px-Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz%2C_Bernhard_Christoph_Francke.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz%2C_Bernhard_Christoph_Francke.jpg/255px-Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz%2C_Bernhard_Christoph_Francke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz%2C_Bernhard_Christoph_Francke.jpg/340px-Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz%2C_Bernhard_Christoph_Francke.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4486" data-file-height="5538" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz,_Bernhard_Christoph_Francke.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Gottfried Leibniz</a><br />(1646–1716)</div></div></div>
<p>Newton's principles (but not his mathematical treatments) proved controversial with Continental philosophers, who found his lack of <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysical</a> explanation for movement and gravitation philosophically unacceptable. Beginning around 1700, a bitter rift opened between the Continental and British philosophical traditions, which were stoked by heated, ongoing, and viciously personal disputes between the followers of Newton and Leibniz concerning priority over the analytical techniques of <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a>, which each had developed independently. Initially, the Cartesian and Leibnizian traditions prevailed on the Continent (leading to the dominance of the Leibnizian calculus notation everywhere except Britain).  Newton himself remained privately disturbed at the lack of a philosophical understanding of gravitation while insisting in his writings that none was necessary to infer its reality.  As the 18th century progressed, Continental natural philosophers increasingly accepted the Newtonians' willingness to forgo <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontological</a> metaphysical explanations for mathematically described motions.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guicciardini1999_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guicciardini1999-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Newton built the first functioning <a href="/wiki/Reflecting_telescope" title="Reflecting telescope">reflecting telescope</a><sup id="cite_ref-Wilson2013_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson2013-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and developed a theory of color, published in <i><a href="/wiki/Opticks" title="Opticks">Opticks</a></i>, based on the observation that a <a href="/wiki/Triangular_prism_(optics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Triangular prism (optics)">prism</a> decomposes <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum#Visible_radiation_(light)" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">white light</a> into the many colours forming the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible spectrum</a>. While Newton explained light as being composed of tiny particles, a rival theory of light which explained its behavior in terms of waves was presented in 1690 by <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a>. However, the belief in the mechanistic philosophy coupled with Newton's reputation meant that the wave theory saw relatively little support until the 19th century. Newton also formulated <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_cooling" title="Newton&#39;s law of cooling">an empirical law of cooling</a>, studied the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a>, investigated <a href="/wiki/Power_series" title="Power series">power series</a>, demonstrated the <a href="/wiki/Binomial_theorem" title="Binomial theorem">generalised binomial theorem</a> and developed <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_method" title="Newton&#39;s method">a method</a> for approximating the <a href="/wiki/Root_of_a_function" class="mw-redirect" title="Root of a function">roots of a function</a>. His work on infinite series was inspired by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Stevin" title="Simon Stevin">Simon Stevin</a>'s decimals.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Most importantly, Newton showed that the motions of objects on Earth and of celestial bodies are governed by the same set of natural laws, which were neither capricious nor malevolent. By demonstrating the consistency between <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler&#39;s laws of planetary motion">Kepler's laws of planetary motion</a> and his own theory of gravitation, Newton also removed the last doubts about heliocentrism. By bringing together all the ideas set forth during the Scientific revolution, Newton effectively established the foundation for modern society in mathematics and science.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_achievements">Other achievements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other achievements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Other branches of physics also received attention during the period of the Scientific revolution. <a href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">William Gilbert</a>, court physician to <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Queen Elizabeth I</a>, published an important work on magnetism in 1600, describing how the earth itself behaves like a giant magnet. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> (1627–91) studied the behavior of gases enclosed in a chamber and formulated the <a href="/wiki/Boyle%27s_law" title="Boyle&#39;s law">gas law named for him</a>; he also contributed to physiology and to the founding of modern chemistry. Another important factor in the scientific revolution was the rise of learned societies and academies in various countries. The earliest of these were in Italy and Germany and were short-lived. More influential were the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Society of England">Royal Society of England</a> (1660) and the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Sciences_(France)" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy of Sciences (France)">Academy of Sciences in France</a> (1666). The former was a private institution in London and included such scientists as <a href="/wiki/John_Wallis" title="John Wallis">John Wallis</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Brouncker,_2nd_Viscount_Brouncker" title="William Brouncker, 2nd Viscount Brouncker">William Brouncker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sydenham" title="Thomas Sydenham">Thomas Sydenham</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Mayow" title="John Mayow">John Mayow</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Wren" title="Christopher Wren">Christopher Wren</a> (who contributed not only to architecture but also to astronomy and anatomy); the latter, in Paris, was a government institution and included as a foreign member the Dutchman Huygens. In the 18th century, important royal academies were established at Berlin (1700) and at St. Petersburg (1724). The societies and academies provided the principal opportunities for the publication and discussion of scientific results during and after the scientific revolution. In 1690, <a href="/wiki/James_Bernoulli" class="mw-redirect" title="James Bernoulli">James Bernoulli</a> showed that the <a href="/wiki/Cycloid" title="Cycloid">cycloid</a> is the solution to the tautochrone problem; and the following year, in 1691, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Bernoulli" title="Johann Bernoulli">Johann Bernoulli</a> showed that a chain freely suspended from two points will form a <a href="/wiki/Catenary" title="Catenary">catenary</a>, the curve with the lowest possible <a href="/wiki/Center_of_gravity" class="mw-redirect" title="Center of gravity">center of gravity</a> available to any chain hung between two fixed points. He then showed, in 1696, that the cycloid is the solution to the <a href="/wiki/Brachistochrone" class="mw-redirect" title="Brachistochrone">brachistochrone</a> problem.
</p>
