<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_thermodynamics">Early thermodynamics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Early thermodynamics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A precursor of the engine was designed by the German scientist <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Guericke" title="Otto von Guericke">Otto von Guericke</a> who, in 1650, designed and built the world's first <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_pump" title="Vacuum pump">vacuum pump</a> and created the world's first ever <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Magdeburg_hemispheres" title="Magdeburg hemispheres">Magdeburg hemispheres</a> experiment.  He was driven to make a vacuum to disprove <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s long-held supposition that <a href="/wiki/Horror_vacui_(physics)" title="Horror vacui (physics)">'Nature abhors a vacuum'</a>. Shortly thereafter, Irish physicist and chemist Boyle had learned of Guericke's designs and in 1656, in coordination with English scientist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a>, built an air pump. Using this pump, Boyle and Hooke noticed the pressure-volume correlation for a gas: <i>PV</i> = <i>k</i>, where <i>P</i> is <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a>, <i>V</i> is <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a> and <i>k</i> is a constant: this relationship is known as <a href="/wiki/Boyle%27s_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="Boyle&#39;s Law">Boyle's Law</a>. In that time, air was assumed to be a system of motionless particles, and not interpreted as a system of moving molecules. The concept of thermal motion came two centuries later.  Therefore, Boyle's publication in 1660 speaks about a mechanical concept: the air spring.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Later, after the invention of the thermometer, the property temperature could be quantified. This tool gave <a href="/wiki/Gay-Lussac" class="mw-redirect" title="Gay-Lussac">Gay-Lussac</a> the opportunity to derive <a href="/wiki/Gay-Lussac%27s_law" title="Gay-Lussac&#39;s law">his law</a>, which led shortly later to the <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas_law" title="Ideal gas law">ideal gas law</a>.  But, already before the establishment of the ideal gas law, an associate of Boyle's named <a href="/wiki/Denis_Papin" title="Denis Papin">Denis Papin</a> built in 1679 a bone digester, which is a closed vessel with a tightly fitting lid that confines steam until a high pressure is generated.
</p><p>Later designs implemented a steam release valve to keep the machine from exploding.  By watching the valve rhythmically move up and down, Papin conceived of the idea of a piston and cylinder engine.  He did not however follow through with his design. Nevertheless, in 1697, based on Papin's designs, engineer <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Savery" title="Thomas Savery">Thomas Savery</a> built the first engine. Although these early engines were crude and inefficient, they attracted the attention of the leading scientists of the time. Hence, prior to 1698 and the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">Savery Engine</a>, horses were used to power pulleys, attached to buckets, which lifted water out of flooded salt mines in England. In the years to follow, more variations of steam engines were built, such as the <a href="/wiki/Newcomen_steam_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Newcomen steam engine">Newcomen Engine</a>, and later the <a href="/wiki/Watt_steam_engine" title="Watt steam engine">Watt Engine</a>. In time, these early engines would eventually be utilized in place of horses.  Thus, each engine began to be associated with a certain amount of "horse power" depending upon how many horses it had replaced. The main problem with these first engines was that they were slow and clumsy, converting less than 2% of the input <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> into useful work.  In other words, large quantities of coal (or wood) had to be burned to yield only a small fraction of work output.  Hence the need for a new science of engine <a href="/wiki/Dynamics_(mechanics)" title="Dynamics (mechanics)">dynamics</a> was born.
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="18th-century_developments">18th-century developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 18th-century developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alessandro_Volta.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Alessandro_Volta.jpeg/220px-Alessandro_Volta.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Alessandro_Volta.jpeg/330px-Alessandro_Volta.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/52/Alessandro_Volta.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="390" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alessandro_Volta.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">Alessandro Volta</a><br />(1745–1827)</div></div></div>
<p>During the 18th century, the mechanics founded by Newton was developed by several scientists as more mathematicians learned calculus and elaborated upon its initial formulation. The application of mathematical analysis to problems of motion was known as rational mechanics, or mixed mathematics (and was later termed <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanics">Mechanics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Mechanics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Danielbernoulli.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Danielbernoulli.jpg/220px-Danielbernoulli.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/de/Danielbernoulli.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="268" data-file-height="326" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Danielbernoulli.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Daniel_Bernoulli" title="Daniel Bernoulli">Daniel Bernoulli</a><br />(1700–1782)</div></div></div>
<p>In 1714, <a href="/wiki/Brook_Taylor" title="Brook Taylor">Brook Taylor</a> derived the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_frequency" title="Fundamental frequency">fundamental frequency</a> of a stretched vibrating string in terms of its tension and mass per unit length by solving a <a href="/wiki/Differential_equation" title="Differential equation">differential equation</a>. The Swiss mathematician <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Bernoulli" title="Daniel Bernoulli">Daniel Bernoulli</a> (1700–1782) made important mathematical studies of the behavior of gases, anticipating the kinetic theory of gases developed more than a century later, and has been referred to as the first mathematical physicist.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In 1733, Daniel Bernoulli derived the fundamental frequency and <a href="/wiki/Harmonic" title="Harmonic">harmonics</a> of a hanging chain by solving a differential equation. In 1734, Bernoulli solved the differential equation for the vibrations of an elastic bar clamped at one end. Bernoulli's treatment of <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">fluid dynamics</a> and his examination of <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluid</a> flow was introduced in his 1738 work <i><a href="/wiki/Hydrodynamica" title="Hydrodynamica">Hydrodynamica</a></i>.
</p><p>Rational mechanics dealt primarily with the development of elaborate mathematical treatments of observed motions, using Newtonian principles as a basis, and emphasized improving the tractability of complex calculations and developing of legitimate means of analytical approximation. A representative contemporary textbook was published by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Baptiste_Horvath" title="Johann Baptiste Horvath">Johann Baptiste Horvath</a>. By the end of the century analytical treatments were rigorous enough to verify the stability of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar system">solar system</a> solely on the basis of Newton's laws without reference to divine intervention—even as deterministic treatments of systems as simple as the <a href="/wiki/N-body_problem" title="N-body problem">three body problem</a> in gravitation remained intractable.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In 1705, <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">Edmond Halley</a> predicted the periodicity of <a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_Comet" title="Halley&#39;s Comet">Halley's Comet</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> discovered <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> in 1781, and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">Henry Cavendish</a> measured the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">gravitational constant</a> and determined the mass of the Earth in 1798. In 1783, <a href="/wiki/John_Michell" title="John Michell">John Michell</a> suggested that some objects might be so massive that not even light could escape from them.
</p><p>In 1739, <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a> solved the ordinary differential equation for a forced harmonic oscillator and noticed the resonance phenomenon. In 1742, <a href="/wiki/Colin_Maclaurin" title="Colin Maclaurin">Colin Maclaurin</a> discovered his <a href="/wiki/Maclaurin_spheroid" title="Maclaurin spheroid">uniformly rotating self-gravitating spheroids</a>. In 1742, Benjamin Robins published his <i>New Principles in Gunnery</i>, establishing the science of aerodynamics. British work, carried on by mathematicians such as Taylor and Maclaurin, fell behind Continental developments as the century progressed. Meanwhile, work flourished at scientific academies on the Continent, led by such mathematicians as Bernoulli, Euler, Lagrange, Laplace, and <a href="/wiki/Adrien-Marie_Legendre" title="Adrien-Marie Legendre">Legendre</a>. In 1743, <a href="/wiki/Jean_le_Rond_d%27Alembert" title="Jean le Rond d&#39;Alembert">Jean le Rond d'Alembert</a> published his <i>Traite de Dynamique</i>, in which he introduced the concept of generalized forces for accelerating systems and systems with constraints, and applied the new idea of <a href="/wiki/Virtual_work" title="Virtual work">virtual work</a> to solve dynamical problem, now known as <a href="/wiki/D%27Alembert%27s_principle" title="D&#39;Alembert&#39;s principle">D'Alembert's principle</a>, as a rival to Newton's second law of motion. In 1747, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Louis_Maupertuis" title="Pierre Louis Maupertuis">Pierre Louis Maupertuis</a> applied minimum principles to mechanics. In 1759, Euler solved the partial differential equation for the vibration of a rectangular drum. In 1764, Euler examined the partial differential equation for the vibration of a circular drum and found one of the Bessel function solutions. In 1776, <a href="/wiki/John_Smeaton" title="John Smeaton">John Smeaton</a> published a paper on experiments relating power, <a href="/wiki/Work_(physics)" title="Work (physics)">work</a>, <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>, and supporting the <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_energy" title="Conservation of energy">conservation of energy</a>. In 1788, Joseph Louis Lagrange presented <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_mechanics" title="Lagrangian mechanics">Lagrange's equations of motion</a> in <i>Mécanique Analytique</i>, in which the whole of mechanics was organized around the principle of virtual work. In 1789, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> states the law of <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_mass" title="Conservation of mass">conservation of mass</a>. The rational mechanics developed in the 18th century received a brilliant exposition in both Lagrange's 1788 work and the <i>Celestial Mechanics</i> (1799–1825) of <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace" title="Pierre-Simon Laplace">Pierre-Simon Laplace</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermodynamics">Thermodynamics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Thermodynamics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the 18th century, thermodynamics was developed through the theories of weightless <a href="/wiki/Imponderable_fluid" title="Imponderable fluid">"imponderable fluids"</a>, such as heat ("caloric"), <a href="/wiki/History_of_electricity" class="mw-redirect" title="History of electricity">electricity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phlogiston_theory" title="Phlogiston theory">phlogiston</a> (which was rapidly overthrown as a concept following <a href="/wiki/Antoine-Laurent_Lavoisier" class="mw-redirect" title="Antoine-Laurent Lavoisier">Lavoisier's</a> identification of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> gas late in the century).  Assuming that these concepts were real fluids, their flow could be traced through a mechanical apparatus or chemical reactions.  This tradition of experimentation led to the development of new kinds of experimental apparatus, such as the <a href="/wiki/Leyden_Jar" class="mw-redirect" title="Leyden Jar">Leyden Jar</a>; and new kinds of measuring instruments, such as the <a href="/wiki/Calorimeter" title="Calorimeter">calorimeter</a>, and improved versions of old ones, such as the <a href="/wiki/Thermometer" title="Thermometer">thermometer</a>.  Experiments also produced new concepts, such as the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Glasgow" title="University of Glasgow">University of Glasgow</a> experimenter <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Black" title="Joseph Black">Joseph Black</a>'s notion of <a href="/wiki/Latent_heat" title="Latent heat">latent heat</a> and Philadelphia intellectual <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>'s characterization of electrical fluid as flowing between places of excess and deficit (a concept later reinterpreted in terms of positive and negative <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">charges</a>). Franklin also showed that lightning is electricity in 1752.
</p><p>The accepted theory of heat in the 18th century viewed it as a kind of fluid, called <a href="/wiki/Caloric_theory" title="Caloric theory">caloric</a>; although this theory was later shown to be erroneous, a number of scientists adhering to it nevertheless made important discoveries useful in developing the modern theory, including <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Black" title="Joseph Black">Joseph Black</a> (1728–99) and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">Henry Cavendish</a> (1731–1810). Opposed to this caloric theory, which had been developed mainly by the chemists, was the less accepted theory dating from Newton's time that heat is due to the motions of the particles of a substance. This mechanical theory gained support in 1798 from the cannon-boring experiments of Count Rumford (<a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Thompson" title="Benjamin Thompson">Benjamin Thompson</a>), who found a direct relationship between heat and mechanical energy.
</p><p>While it was recognized early in the 18th century that finding absolute theories of electrostatic and magnetic force akin to Newton's principles of motion would be an important achievement, none were forthcoming.  This impossibility only slowly disappeared as experimental practice became more widespread and more refined in the early years of the 19th century in places such as the newly established <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">Royal Institution</a> in London. Meanwhile, the analytical methods of rational mechanics began to be applied to experimental phenomena, most influentially with the French mathematician <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Fourier" title="Joseph Fourier">Joseph Fourier</a>'s analytical treatment of the flow of heat, as published in 1822.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> proposed an electrical inverse-square law in 1767, and <a href="/wiki/Charles-Augustin_de_Coulomb" title="Charles-Augustin de Coulomb">Charles-Augustin de Coulomb</a> introduced the inverse-square law of <a href="/wiki/Electrostatics" title="Electrostatics">electrostatics</a> in 1798.
</p><p>At the end of the century, the members of the <a href="/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences" title="French Academy of Sciences">French Academy of Sciences</a> had attained clear dominance in the field.<sup id="cite_ref-Guicciardini1999_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guicciardini1999-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, the experimental tradition established by Galileo and his followers persisted.  The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> and the <a href="/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences" title="French Academy of Sciences">French Academy of Sciences</a> were major centers for the performance and reporting of experimental work. Experiments in mechanics, optics, <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Static_electricity" title="Static electricity">static electricity</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_chemistry" title="History of chemistry">chemistry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a> were not clearly distinguished from each other during the 18th century, but significant differences in explanatory schemes and, thus, experiment design were emerging.  Chemical experimenters, for instance, defied attempts to enforce a scheme of abstract Newtonian forces onto chemical affiliations, and instead focused on the isolation and classification of chemical substances and reactions.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Faraday-Millikan-Gale-1913.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Faraday-Millikan-Gale-1913.jpg/170px-Faraday-Millikan-Gale-1913.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Faraday-Millikan-Gale-1913.jpg/255px-Faraday-Millikan-Gale-1913.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Faraday-Millikan-Gale-1913.jpg/340px-Faraday-Millikan-Gale-1913.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1408" data-file-height="1880" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Faraday-Millikan-Gale-1913.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a><br />(1791–1867)</div></div></div>
<p>In 1800, <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">Alessandro Volta</a> invented the electric battery (known of the <a href="/wiki/Voltaic_pile" title="Voltaic pile">voltaic pile</a>) and thus improved the way electric currents could also be studied. A year later, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Young_(scientist)" title="Thomas Young (scientist)">Thomas Young</a> demonstrated the wave nature of light—which received strong experimental support from the work of <a href="/wiki/Augustin-Jean_Fresnel" title="Augustin-Jean Fresnel">Augustin-Jean Fresnel</a>—and the principle of interference. In 1813, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Ewart" title="Peter Ewart">Peter Ewart</a> supported the idea of the conservation of energy in his paper <i>On the measure of moving force</i>. In 1820, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted" title="Hans Christian Ørsted">Hans Christian Ørsted</a> found that a current-carrying conductor gives rise to a magnetic force surrounding it, and within a week after Ørsted's discovery reached France, <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9-Marie_Amp%C3%A8re" title="André-Marie Ampère">André-Marie Ampère</a> discovered that two parallel electric currents will exert forces on each other. In 1821, <a href="/wiki/William_Rowan_Hamilton" title="William Rowan Hamilton">William Hamilton</a> began his analysis of Hamilton's characteristic function. In 1821, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a> built an electricity-powered motor, while <a href="/wiki/Georg_Ohm" title="Georg Ohm">Georg Ohm</a> stated his law of electrical resistance in 1826, expressing the relationship between voltage, current, and resistance in an electric circuit. A year later, botanist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Brown_(Scottish_botanist_from_Montrose)" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Brown (Scottish botanist from Montrose)">Robert Brown</a> discovered <a href="/wiki/Brownian_motion" title="Brownian motion">Brownian motion</a>: pollen grains in water undergoing movement resulting from their bombardment by the fast-moving atoms or molecules in the liquid.  In 1829, <a href="/wiki/Gaspard-Gustave_Coriolis" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaspard-Gustave Coriolis">Gaspard Coriolis</a> introduced the terms of <a href="/wiki/Work_(physics)" title="Work (physics)">work</a> (force times distance) and <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> with the meanings they have today.
</p><p>In 1831, Faraday (and independently <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Henry" title="Joseph Henry">Joseph Henry</a>) discovered the reverse effect, the production of an electric potential or current through magnetism&#160;– known as <a href="/wiki/Faraday%27s_law_of_induction" title="Faraday&#39;s law of induction">electromagnetic induction</a>; these two discoveries are the basis of the electric motor and the electric generator, respectively. In 1834, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Gustav_Jakob_Jacobi" class="mw-redirect" title="Carl Gustav Jakob Jacobi">Carl Jacobi</a> discovered his uniformly rotating self-gravitating ellipsoids (the <a href="/wiki/Jacobi_ellipsoid" title="Jacobi ellipsoid">Jacobi ellipsoid</a>). In 1834, <a href="/wiki/John_Scott_Russell" title="John Scott Russell">John Russell</a> observed a nondecaying solitary water wave (<a href="/wiki/Soliton" title="Soliton">soliton</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/Union_Canal_(Scotland)" title="Union Canal (Scotland)">Union Canal</a> near <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a> and used a water tank to study the dependence of solitary water wave velocities on wave amplitude and water depth. In 1835, William Hamilton stated <a href="/wiki/Hamiltonian_mechanics" title="Hamiltonian mechanics">Hamilton's canonical equations of motion</a>. In the same year, <a href="/wiki/Gaspard-Gustave_Coriolis" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaspard-Gustave Coriolis">Gaspard Coriolis</a> examined theoretically the mechanical efficiency of waterwheels, and deduced the <a href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a>. In 1841, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Robert_von_Mayer" class="mw-redirect" title="Julius Robert von Mayer">Julius Robert von Mayer</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Amateur" title="Amateur">amateur</a> scientist, wrote a paper on the conservation of energy but his lack of academic training led to its rejection. In 1842, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Doppler" title="Christian Doppler">Christian Doppler</a> proposed the <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler effect</a>. In 1847, <a href="/wiki/Hermann_von_Helmholtz" title="Hermann von Helmholtz">Hermann von Helmholtz</a> formally stated the law of conservation of energy. In 1851, <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Foucault" title="Léon Foucault">Léon Foucault</a> showed the Earth's rotation with a huge <a href="/wiki/Pendulum" title="Pendulum">pendulum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Foucault_pendulum" title="Foucault pendulum">Foucault pendulum</a>).
</p><p>There were important advances in <a href="/wiki/Continuum_mechanics" title="Continuum mechanics">continuum mechanics</a> in the first half of the century, namely formulation of <a href="/wiki/Elastic_modulus" title="Elastic modulus">laws of elasticity</a> for solids and discovery of <a href="/wiki/Navier%E2%80%93Stokes_equations" title="Navier–Stokes equations">Navier–Stokes equations</a> for fluids.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws_of_thermodynamics">Laws of thermodynamics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Laws of thermodynamics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_thermodynamics" title="History of thermodynamics">History of thermodynamics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lord_Kelvin_photograph.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Lord_Kelvin_photograph.jpg/200px-Lord_Kelvin_photograph.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Lord_Kelvin_photograph.jpg/300px-Lord_Kelvin_photograph.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Lord_Kelvin_photograph.jpg/400px-Lord_Kelvin_photograph.jpg 2x" data-file-width="939" data-file-height="1176" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lord_Kelvin_photograph.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">William Thomson (Lord Kelvin)</a><br />(1824–1907)</span></div></div></div>
<p>In the 19th century, the connection between heat and mechanical energy was established quantitatively by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Robert_von_Mayer" class="mw-redirect" title="Julius Robert von Mayer">Julius Robert von Mayer</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Prescott_Joule" title="James Prescott Joule">James Prescott Joule</a>, who measured the mechanical equivalent of heat in the 1840s. In 1849, Joule published results from his series of experiments (including the paddlewheel experiment) which show that heat is a form of energy, a fact that was accepted in the 1850s. The relation between heat and energy was important for the development of steam engines, and in 1824 the experimental and theoretical work of <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_L%C3%A9onard_Sadi_Carnot" title="Nicolas Léonard Sadi Carnot">Sadi Carnot</a> was published. Carnot captured some of the ideas of thermodynamics in his discussion of the efficiency of an idealized engine. Sadi Carnot's work provided a basis for the formulation of the <a href="/wiki/First_law_of_thermodynamics" title="First law of thermodynamics">first law of thermodynamics</a>—a restatement of the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_conservation_of_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Law of conservation of energy">law of conservation of energy</a>—which was stated around 1850 by <a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">William Thomson</a>, later known as Lord Kelvin, and <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Clausius" title="Rudolf Clausius">Rudolf Clausius</a>. Lord Kelvin, who had extended the concept of absolute zero from gases to all substances in 1848, drew upon the engineering theory of <a href="/wiki/Lazare_Carnot" title="Lazare Carnot">Lazare Carnot</a>, Sadi Carnot, and <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Clapeyron" class="mw-redirect" title="Émile Clapeyron">Émile Clapeyron</a>–as well as the experimentation of James Prescott Joule on the interchangeability of mechanical, chemical, thermal, and electrical forms of work—to formulate the first law.
</p><p>Kelvin and Clausius also stated the <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a>, which was originally formulated in terms of the fact that heat does not spontaneously flow from a colder body to a hotter. Other formulations followed quickly (for example, the second law was expounded in Thomson and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Guthrie_Tait" class="mw-redirect" title="Peter Guthrie Tait">Peter Guthrie Tait</a>'s influential work <i>Treatise on Natural Philosophy</i>) and Kelvin in particular understood some of the law's general implications. The second Law was the idea that gases consist of molecules in motion had been discussed in some detail by Daniel Bernoulli in 1738, but had fallen out of favor, and was revived by Clausius in 1857. In 1850, <a href="/wiki/Hippolyte_Fizeau" title="Hippolyte Fizeau">Hippolyte Fizeau</a> and <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Foucault" title="Léon Foucault">Léon Foucault</a> measured the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> in water and find that it is slower than in air, in support of the wave model of light. In 1852, Joule and Thomson demonstrated that a rapidly expanding gas cools, later named the <a href="/wiki/Joule%E2%80%93Thomson_effect" title="Joule–Thomson effect">Joule–Thomson effect</a> or Joule–Kelvin effect. <a href="/wiki/Hermann_von_Helmholtz" title="Hermann von Helmholtz">Hermann von Helmholtz</a> puts forward the idea of the <a href="/wiki/Heat_death_of_the_universe" title="Heat death of the universe">heat death of the universe</a> in 1854, the same year that Clausius established the importance of <i>dQ/T</i> (<a href="/wiki/Clausius%27s_theorem" class="mw-redirect" title="Clausius&#39;s theorem">Clausius's theorem</a>) (though he did not yet name the quantity).
</p>
<h3><span id="Statistical_mechanics_.28a_fundamentally_new_approach_to_science.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistical_mechanics_(a_fundamentally_new_approach_to_science)">Statistical mechanics (a fundamentally new approach to science)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Statistical mechanics (a fundamentally new approach to science)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_statistical_mechanics" class="mw-redirect" title="History of statistical mechanics">History of statistical mechanics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Clerk_Maxwell.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/James_Clerk_Maxwell.png/180px-James_Clerk_Maxwell.png" decoding="async" width="180" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/James_Clerk_Maxwell.png/270px-James_Clerk_Maxwell.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/57/James_Clerk_Maxwell.png 2x" data-file-width="331" data-file-height="398" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Clerk_Maxwell.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a><br />(1831–1879)</div></div></div>
<p>In 1859, <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> discovered the <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution">distribution law of molecular velocities</a>. Maxwell showed that electric and magnetic fields are propagated outward from their source at a speed equal to that of light and that light is one of several kinds of electromagnetic radiation, differing only in frequency and wavelength from the others. In 1859, Maxwell worked out the mathematics of the distribution of velocities of the molecules of a gas. The wave theory of light was widely accepted by the time of Maxwell's work on the electromagnetic field, and afterward the study of light and that of electricity and magnetism were closely related. In 1864 James Maxwell published his papers on a dynamical theory of the electromagnetic field, and stated that light is an electromagnetic phenomenon in the 1873 publication of Maxwell's <i><a href="/wiki/A_Treatise_on_Electricity_and_Magnetism" title="A Treatise on Electricity and Magnetism">Treatise on Electricity and Magnetism</a></i>. This work drew upon theoretical work by German theoreticians such as <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Eduard_Weber" title="Wilhelm Eduard Weber">Wilhelm Weber</a>. The encapsulation of heat in particulate motion, and the addition of electromagnetic forces to Newtonian dynamics established an enormously robust theoretical underpinning to physical observations.
</p><p>The prediction that light represented a transmission of energy in wave form through a "<a href="/wiki/Luminiferous_aether" title="Luminiferous aether">luminiferous ether</a>", and the seeming confirmation of that prediction with Helmholtz student <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Hertz" title="Heinrich Hertz">Heinrich Hertz</a>'s 1888 detection of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a>, was a major triumph for physical theory and raised the possibility that even more fundamental theories based on the field could soon be developed.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Experimental confirmation of Maxwell's theory was provided by Hertz, who generated and detected electric waves in 1886 and verified their properties, at the same time foreshadowing their application in radio, television, and other devices. In 1887, Heinrich Hertz discovered the <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric effect</a>. Research on the electromagnetic waves began soon after, with many scientists and inventors conducting experiments on their properties. In the mid to late 1890s <a href="/wiki/Guglielmo_Marconi" title="Guglielmo Marconi">Guglielmo Marconi</a> developed a <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio wave</a> based <a href="/wiki/Wireless_telegraphy" title="Wireless telegraphy">wireless telegraphy</a> system<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> (see <a href="/wiki/Invention_of_radio" title="Invention of radio">invention of radio</a>).
</p><p>The atomic theory of matter had been proposed again in the early 19th century by the chemist <a href="/wiki/John_Dalton" title="John Dalton">John Dalton</a> and became one of the hypotheses of the kinetic-molecular theory of gases developed by Clausius and James Clerk Maxwell to explain the laws of thermodynamics.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boltzmann2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Boltzmann2.jpg/180px-Boltzmann2.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Boltzmann2.jpg/270px-Boltzmann2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Boltzmann2.jpg/360px-Boltzmann2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="490" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boltzmann2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann" title="Ludwig Boltzmann">Ludwig Boltzmann</a><br />(1844-1906)</div></div></div>
<p>The kinetic theory in turn led to a revolutionary approach to science, the <a href="/wiki/Statistical_mechanics" title="Statistical mechanics">statistical mechanics</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann" title="Ludwig Boltzmann">Ludwig Boltzmann</a> (1844–1906) and <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Willard_Gibbs" title="Josiah Willard Gibbs">Josiah Willard Gibbs</a> (1839–1903), which studies the statistics of microstates of a system and uses statistics to determine the state of a physical system.  Interrelating the statistical likelihood of certain states of organization of these particles with the energy of those states, Clausius reinterpreted the dissipation of energy to be the statistical tendency of molecular configurations to pass toward increasingly likely, increasingly disorganized states (coining the term "<a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a>" to describe the disorganization of a state). The statistical versus absolute interpretations of the second law of thermodynamics set up a dispute that would last for several decades (producing arguments such as "<a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_demon" title="Maxwell&#39;s demon">Maxwell's demon</a>"), and that would not be held to be definitively resolved until the behavior of atoms was firmly established in the early 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> In 1902, <a href="/wiki/James_Jeans" title="James Jeans">James Jeans</a> found the length scale required for gravitational perturbations to grow in a static nearly homogeneous medium.
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century:_birth_of_modern_physics">20th century: birth of modern physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 20th century: birth of modern physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mariecurie.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Mariecurie.jpg/200px-Mariecurie.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Mariecurie.jpg/300px-Mariecurie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Mariecurie.jpg/400px-Mariecurie.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1987" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mariecurie.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie Skłodowska-Curie</a><br />(1867–1934)</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Modern_physics" title="Modern physics">Modern physics</a></div>
<p>At the end of the 19th century, physics had evolved to the point at which <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a> could cope with highly complex problems involving macroscopic situations; thermodynamics and kinetic theory were well established; geometrical and physical optics could be understood in terms of electromagnetic waves; and the conservation laws for energy and momentum (and mass) were widely accepted. So profound were these and other developments that it was generally accepted that all the important laws of physics had been discovered and that, henceforth, research would be concerned with clearing up minor problems and particularly with improvements of method and measurement. However, around 1900 serious doubts arose about the completeness of the classical theories—the triumph of Maxwell's theories, for example, was undermined by inadequacies that had already begun to appear—and their inability to explain certain physical phenomena, such as the energy distribution in <a href="/wiki/Blackbody_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Blackbody radiation">blackbody radiation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric effect</a>, while some of the theoretical formulations led to paradoxes when pushed to the limit. Prominent physicists such as <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Lorentz" title="Hendrik Lorentz">Hendrik Lorentz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emil_Cohn" title="Emil Cohn">Emil Cohn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Wiechert" title="Ernst Wiechert">Ernst Wiechert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Wien" title="Wilhelm Wien">Wilhelm Wien</a> believed that some modification of <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell&#39;s equations">Maxwell's equations</a> might provide the basis for all physical laws. These shortcomings of <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">classical physics</a> were never to be resolved and new ideas were required. At the beginning of the 20th century a major revolution shook the world of physics, which led to a new era, generally referred to as <a href="/wiki/Modern_physics" title="Modern physics">modern physics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Harvtxt&#124;Agar&#124;2012_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvtxt|Agar|2012-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation_experiments">Radiation experiments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Radiation experiments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:J.J_Thomson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/J.J_Thomson.jpg/200px-J.J_Thomson.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="313" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/J.J_Thomson.jpg/300px-J.J_Thomson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/J.J_Thomson.jpg/400px-J.J_Thomson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1563" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:J.J_Thomson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">J. J. Thomson</a> (1856–1940) discovered the <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopy</a> and also invented the <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometer" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass spectrometer">mass spectrometer</a>. He was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a> in 1906.</div></div></div>
<p>In the 19th century, experimenters began to detect unexpected forms of radiation: <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_R%C3%B6ntgen" title="Wilhelm Röntgen">Wilhelm Röntgen</a> caused a sensation with his discovery of <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> in 1895; in 1896 <a href="/wiki/Henri_Becquerel" title="Henri Becquerel">Henri Becquerel</a> discovered that certain kinds of matter emit radiation on their own accord. In 1897, <a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">J. J. Thomson</a> discovered the <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>, and new radioactive elements found by <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">Pierre Curie</a> raised questions about the supposedly indestructible atom and the nature of matter. Marie and Pierre coined the term "<a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactivity</a>" to describe this property of matter, and isolated the radioactive elements <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">polonium</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Soddy" title="Frederick Soddy">Frederick Soddy</a> identified two of Becquerel's forms of radiation with electrons and the element <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>. Rutherford identified and named two types of radioactivity and in 1911 interpreted experimental evidence as showing that the atom consists of a dense, positively charged nucleus surrounded by negatively charged electrons. Classical theory, however, predicted that this structure should be unstable. Classical theory had also failed to explain successfully two other experimental results that appeared in the late 19th century. One of these was the demonstration by <a href="/wiki/Albert_A._Michelson" title="Albert A. Michelson">Albert A. Michelson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edward_W._Morley" title="Edward W. Morley">Edward W. Morley</a>—known as the <a href="/wiki/Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment" title="Michelson–Morley experiment">Michelson–Morley experiment</a>—which showed there did not seem to be a preferred frame of reference, at rest with respect to the hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Luminiferous_ether" class="mw-redirect" title="Luminiferous ether">luminiferous ether</a>, for describing electromagnetic phenomena. Studies of radiation and radioactive decay continued to be a preeminent focus for physical and chemical research through the 1930s, when the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> opened the way to the practical exploitation of what came to be called <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">"atomic" energy</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Albert_Einstein.27s_theory_of_relativity"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Albert_Einstein's_theory_of_relativity">Albert Einstein's theory of relativity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Albert Einstein&#039;s theory of relativity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1905 a young, 26-year-old German physicist (then a Bern patent clerk) named <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> (1879–1955), showed how measurements of time and space are affected by motion between an observer and what is being observed. To say that Einstein's radical theory of relativity revolutionized science is no exaggeration. Although Einstein made many other important contributions to science, the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_relativity" title="Theory of relativity">theory of relativity</a> alone represents one of the greatest intellectual achievements of all time. Although the concept of relativity was not introduced by Einstein, his major contribution was the recognition that the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> in a vacuum is constant, i.e. the same for all observers,  and an absolute physical boundary for motion. This does not impact a person's day-to-day life since most objects travel at speeds much slower than light speed. For objects travelling near light speed, however, the theory of relativity shows that clocks associated with those objects will run more slowly and that the objects shorten in length according to measurements of an observer on Earth. Einstein also derived the famous equation, <i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup>, which expresses the <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass–energy equivalence">equivalence of mass and energy</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_relativity">Special relativity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Special relativity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_special_relativity" title="History of special relativity">History of special relativity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Albert_Einstein_Head.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Albert_Einstein_Head.jpg/220px-Albert_Einstein_Head.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Albert_Einstein_Head.jpg/330px-Albert_Einstein_Head.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Albert_Einstein_Head.jpg/440px-Albert_Einstein_Head.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3250" data-file-height="4333" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Albert_Einstein_Head.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> (1879–1955) proposed that <a href="/wiki/Gravitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitation">gravitation</a> was a result of <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">masses</a> (or their <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass–energy equivalence">equivalent energies</a>) <a href="/wiki/Curvature_of_spacetime" class="mw-redirect" title="Curvature of spacetime">curving ("bending")</a> the <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a> in which they exist, altering the paths they follow within it.</div></div></div>
<p>Einstein argued that the speed of light was a constant in all <a href="/wiki/Inertial_frame_of_reference" title="Inertial frame of reference">inertial reference frames</a> and that electromagnetic laws should remain valid independent of reference frame—assertions which rendered the ether "superfluous" to physical theory, and that held that observations of time and length varied relative to how the observer was moving with respect to the object being measured (what came to be called the "<a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special theory of relativity</a>").  It also followed that mass and energy were interchangeable quantities according to the equation <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass–energy equivalence"><i>E</i>=<i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></a>.  In another paper published the same year, Einstein asserted that electromagnetic radiation was transmitted in discrete quantities ("<a href="/wiki/Quantum" title="Quantum">quanta</a>"), according to a constant that the theoretical physicist <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">Max Planck</a> had posited in 1900 to arrive at an accurate theory for the distribution of <a href="/wiki/Blackbody_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Blackbody radiation">blackbody radiation</a>—an assumption that explained the strange properties of the <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric effect</a>.
</p><p>The special theory of relativity is a formulation of the relationship between physical observations and the concepts of space and time. The theory arose out of contradictions between electromagnetism and Newtonian mechanics and had great impact on both those areas. The original historical issue was whether it was meaningful to discuss the electromagnetic wave-carrying "ether" and motion relative to it and also whether one could detect such motion, as was unsuccessfully attempted in the Michelson–Morley experiment. Einstein demolished these questions and the ether concept in his special theory of relativity. However, his basic formulation does not involve detailed electromagnetic theory. It arises out of the question: "What is time?" Newton, in the <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a></i> (1686), had given an unambiguous answer: "Absolute, true, and mathematical time, of itself, and from its own nature, flows equably without relation to anything external, and by another name is called duration." This definition is basic to all classical physics.
</p><p>Einstein had the genius to question it, and found that it was incomplete. Instead, each "observer" necessarily makes use of his or her own scale of time, and for two observers in relative motion, their time-scales will differ. This induces a related effect on position measurements. Space and time become intertwined concepts, fundamentally dependent on the observer. Each observer presides over his or her own space-time framework or coordinate system. There being no absolute frame of reference, all observers of given events make different but equally valid (and reconcilable) measurements.  What remains absolute is stated in Einstein's relativity postulate: "The basic laws of physics are identical for two observers who have a constant relative velocity with respect to each other."
</p><p>Special Relativity had a profound effect on physics: started as a rethinking of the theory of electromagnetism, it found a new <a href="/wiki/Symmetry_(physics)" title="Symmetry (physics)">symmetry law</a> of nature, now called <i><a href="/wiki/Poincar%C3%A9_symmetry" class="mw-redirect" title="Poincaré symmetry">Poincaré symmetry</a></i>, that replaced the old Galilean (see <a href="#Galileo_Galilei">above</a>) symmetry.
</p><p>Special Relativity exerted another long-lasting effect on <a href="/wiki/Dynamics_(physics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dynamics (physics)">dynamics</a>. Although initially it was credited with the "unification of mass and energy", it became evident that <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_dynamics" title="Relativistic dynamics">relativistic dynamics</a> established a firm <i>distinction</i> between <a href="/wiki/Rest_mass" class="mw-redirect" title="Rest mass">rest mass</a>, which is an invariant (observer independent) property of a <a href="/wiki/Particle" title="Particle">particle</a> or system of particles, and the <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> of a system.  The latter two are separately <a href="/wiki/Conservation_law_(physics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservation law (physics)">conserved</a> in all situations but not invariant with respect to different observers. The term <i><a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a> underwent a <a href="/wiki/Semantic_change" title="Semantic change">semantic change</a>, and since the late 20th century it almost exclusively denotes the <a href="/wiki/Invariant_mass" title="Invariant mass">rest (or <i>invariant</i>) mass</a>. See <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_special_relativity" title="Mass in special relativity">mass in special relativity</a> for additional discussion.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="General_relativity">General relativity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: General relativity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_general_relativity" title="History of general relativity">History of general relativity</a></div>
<p>By 1916, Einstein was able to generalize this further, to deal with all states of motion including non-uniform acceleration, which became the general theory of relativity. In this theory Einstein also specified a new concept, the curvature of space-time, which described the gravitational effect at every point in space. In fact, the curvature of space-time completely replaced Newton's universal law of gravitation. According to Einstein, gravitational force in the normal sense is a kind of illusion caused by the geometry of space. The presence of a mass causes a curvature of space-time in the vicinity of the mass, and this curvature dictates the space-time path that all freely-moving objects must follow. It was also predicted from this theory that light should be subject to gravity - all of which was verified experimentally. This aspect of relativity explained the phenomena of light bending around the sun, predicted black holes as well as properties of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">Cosmic microwave background radiation</a> — a discovery rendering fundamental anomalies in the classic Steady-State hypothesis. For his work on relativity, the photoelectric effect and blackbody radiation, Einstein received the Nobel Prize in 1921.
</p><p>The gradual acceptance of Einstein's theories of relativity and the quantized nature of light transmission, and of <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr%27s_model_of_the_atom" class="mw-redirect" title="Niels Bohr&#39;s model of the atom">Niels Bohr's model of the atom</a> created as many problems as they solved, leading to a full-scale effort to reestablish physics on new fundamental principles.  Expanding relativity to cases of accelerating reference frames (the "<a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general theory of relativity</a>") in the 1910s, Einstein posited an equivalence between the inertial force of acceleration and the force of gravity, leading to the conclusion that space is curved and finite in size, and the prediction of such phenomena as <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lens" title="Gravitational lens">gravitational lensing</a> and the distortion of time in gravitational fields.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_mechanics">Quantum mechanics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Quantum mechanics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_quantum_mechanics" title="History of quantum mechanics">History of quantum mechanics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Max_Planck_1878.GIF" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Max_Planck_1878.GIF/180px-Max_Planck_1878.GIF" decoding="async" width="180" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Max_Planck_1878.GIF/270px-Max_Planck_1878.GIF 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/62/Max_Planck_1878.GIF 2x" data-file-width="332" data-file-height="441" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Max_Planck_1878.GIF" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">Max Planck</a><br />(1858–1947)</div></div></div>
<p>Although relativity resolved the electromagnetic phenomena conflict demonstrated by Michelson and Morley, a second theoretical problem was the explanation of the distribution of electromagnetic radiation emitted by a <a href="/wiki/Black_body" title="Black body">black body</a>; experiment showed that at shorter wavelengths, toward the ultraviolet end of the spectrum, the energy approached zero, but classical theory predicted it should become infinite. This glaring discrepancy, known as the <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_catastrophe" title="Ultraviolet catastrophe">ultraviolet catastrophe</a>, was solved by the new theory of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>. Quantum mechanics is the theory of <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> and subatomic systems. Approximately the first 30 years of the 20th century represent the time of the conception and evolution of the theory. The basic ideas of quantum theory were introduced in 1900 by <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">Max Planck</a> (1858–1947), who was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_for_Physics" class="mw-redirect" title="Nobel Prize for Physics">Nobel Prize for Physics</a> in 1918 for his discovery of the quantified nature of energy. The quantum theory (which previously relied in the "correspondence" at large scales between the quantized world of the atom and the continuities of the "<a href="/wiki/Physics_in_the_Classical_Limit" class="mw-redirect" title="Physics in the Classical Limit">classical</a>" world) was accepted when the <a href="/wiki/Compton_Effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Compton Effect">Compton Effect</a> established that light carries momentum and can scatter off particles, and when <a href="/wiki/Louis_de_Broglie" title="Louis de Broglie">Louis de Broglie</a> asserted that matter can be seen as behaving as a wave in much the same way as electromagnetic waves behave like particles (<a href="/wiki/Wave%E2%80%93particle_duality" title="Wave–particle duality">wave–particle duality</a>).
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heisenberg_10.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Heisenberg_10.jpg/180px-Heisenberg_10.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Heisenberg_10.jpg/270px-Heisenberg_10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Heisenberg_10.jpg/360px-Heisenberg_10.jpg 2x" data-file-width="542" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heisenberg_10.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg" title="Werner Heisenberg">Werner Heisenberg</a><br />(1901–1976)</div></div></div>
<p>In 1905, Einstein used the quantum theory to explain the photoelectric effect, and in 1913 the Danish physicist <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a> used the same constant to explain the stability of <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_model" title="Rutherford model">Rutherford's atom</a> as well as the frequencies of light emitted by hydrogen gas. The quantized theory of the atom gave way to a full-scale quantum mechanics in the 1920s. New principles of a "quantum" rather than a "classical" mechanics, formulated in <a href="/wiki/Matrix_mechanics" title="Matrix mechanics">matrix-form</a> by <a href="/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg" title="Werner Heisenberg">Werner Heisenberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Max_Born" title="Max Born">Max Born</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pascual_Jordan" title="Pascual Jordan">Pascual Jordan</a> in 1925, were based on the probabilistic relationship between discrete "states" and denied the possibility of <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">causality</a>. Quantum mechanics was extensively developed by Heisenberg, <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Pauli" title="Wolfgang Pauli">Wolfgang Pauli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Dirac" title="Paul Dirac">Paul Dirac</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Schr%C3%B6dinger" title="Erwin Schrödinger">Erwin Schrödinger</a>, who established an equivalent theory based on waves in 1926; but Heisenberg's 1927 "<a href="/wiki/Uncertainty_principle" title="Uncertainty principle">uncertainty principle</a>" (indicating the impossibility of precisely and simultaneously measuring position and <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a>) and the "<a href="/wiki/Copenhagen_interpretation" title="Copenhagen interpretation">Copenhagen interpretation</a>" of quantum mechanics (named after Bohr's home city) continued to deny the possibility of fundamental causality, though opponents such as Einstein would metaphorically assert that "God does not play dice with the universe".<sup id="cite_ref-Harvtxt&#124;Kragh&#124;1999_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvtxt|Kragh|1999-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The new quantum mechanics became an indispensable tool in the investigation and explanation of phenomena at the atomic level. Also in the 1920s, the Indian scientist <a href="/wiki/Satyendra_Nath_Bose" title="Satyendra Nath Bose">Satyendra Nath Bose</a>'s work on <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> and quantum mechanics provided the foundation for <a href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_statistics" title="Bose–Einstein statistics">Bose–Einstein statistics</a>, the theory of the <a href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_condensate" title="Bose–Einstein condensate">Bose–Einstein condensate</a>.
</p><p><span id="spin–statistics"></span> The <a href="/wiki/Spin%E2%80%93statistics_theorem" title="Spin–statistics theorem">spin–statistics theorem</a> established that any particle in quantum mechanics may be either a <a href="/wiki/Boson" title="Boson">boson</a> (statistically Bose–Einstein) or a <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermion</a> (statistically <a href="/wiki/Fermi%E2%80%93Dirac_statistics" title="Fermi–Dirac statistics">Fermi–Dirac</a>). It was later found that all <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">fundamental</a> bosons transmit forces, such as the photon that transmits electromagnetism.
</p><p>Fermions are particles "like electrons and nucleons" and are the usual constituents of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a>. Fermi–Dirac statistics later found numerous other uses, from astrophysics (see <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_matter" title="Degenerate matter">Degenerate matter</a>) to <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> design.
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_and_particle_physics">Contemporary and particle physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Contemporary and particle physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_subatomic_physics" title="History of subatomic physics">History of subatomic physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="List of unsolved problems in physics">List of unsolved problems in physics</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_field_theory">Quantum field theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Quantum field theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:289px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Feynmann_Diagram_Gluon_Radiation.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Feynmann_Diagram_Gluon_Radiation.svg/287px-Feynmann_Diagram_Gluon_Radiation.svg.png" decoding="async" width="287" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Feynmann_Diagram_Gluon_Radiation.svg/431px-Feynmann_Diagram_Gluon_Radiation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Feynmann_Diagram_Gluon_Radiation.svg/574px-Feynmann_Diagram_Gluon_Radiation.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="279" data-file-height="178" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Feynmann_Diagram_Gluon_Radiation.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Feynman_diagram" title="Feynman diagram">Feynman diagram</a> representing (left to right) the production of a photon (blue <a href="/wiki/Sine_wave" title="Sine wave">sine wave</a>) from the <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">annihilation</a> of an electron and its complementary <a href="/wiki/Antiparticle" title="Antiparticle">antiparticle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a>. The photon becomes a <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quark</a>–<a href="/wiki/Antiquark" class="mw-redirect" title="Antiquark">antiquark</a> pair and a <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluon</a> (green spiral) is released.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_Feynman_ID_badge.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/41/Richard_Feynman_ID_badge.png" decoding="async" width="130" height="180" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="130" data-file-height="180" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_Feynman_ID_badge.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Richard_Feynman" title="Richard Feynman">Richard Feynman</a>'s Los Alamos ID badge</div></div></div>
<p>As the philosophically inclined continued to debate the fundamental nature of the universe, quantum theories continued to be produced, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Paul_Dirac" title="Paul Dirac">Paul Dirac</a>'s formulation of a relativistic quantum theory in 1928.  However, attempts to quantize electromagnetic theory entirely were stymied throughout the 1930s by theoretical formulations yielding infinite energies. This situation was not considered adequately resolved until after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War&#160;II</a> ended, when <a href="/wiki/Julian_Schwinger" title="Julian Schwinger">Julian Schwinger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Feynman" title="Richard Feynman">Richard Feynman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sin-Itiro_Tomonaga" class="mw-redirect" title="Sin-Itiro Tomonaga">Sin-Itiro Tomonaga</a> independently posited the technique of <a href="/wiki/Renormalization" title="Renormalization">renormalization</a>, which allowed for an establishment of a robust <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">quantum electrodynamics</a> (QED).<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, new theories of <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">fundamental particles</a> proliferated with the rise of the idea of the <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantization of fields</a> through "<a href="/wiki/Exchange_interaction" title="Exchange interaction">exchange forces</a>" regulated by an exchange of short-lived <a href="/wiki/Virtual_particle" title="Virtual particle">"virtual" particles</a>, which were allowed to exist according to the laws governing the uncertainties inherent in the quantum world. Notably, <a href="/wiki/Hideki_Yukawa" title="Hideki Yukawa">Hideki Yukawa</a> proposed that the positive charges of the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> were kept together courtesy of a powerful but short-range force mediated by a particle with a mass between that of the <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a>. This particle, the "<a href="/wiki/Pion" title="Pion">pion</a>", was identified in 1947 as part of what became a slew of particles discovered after World War II. Initially, such particles were found as <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionizing radiation</a> left by <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>, but increasingly came to be produced in newer and more powerful <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerators</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside particle physics, significant advances of the time were:
</p>
<ul><li>the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a> (1964 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a>);</li>
<li>the theoretical and experimental research of <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconductivity</a>, especially the invention of a <a href="/wiki/Ginzburg%E2%80%93Landau_theory" title="Ginzburg–Landau theory">quantum theory of superconductivity</a> by <a href="/wiki/Vitaly_Ginzburg" title="Vitaly Ginzburg">Vitaly Ginzburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lev_Landau" title="Lev Landau">Lev Landau</a> (1962 Nobel Prize in Physics) and, later, its explanation via <a href="/wiki/Cooper_pair" title="Cooper pair">Cooper pairs</a> (1972 Nobel Prize in Physics). The Cooper pair was an early example of <a href="/wiki/Quasiparticle" title="Quasiparticle">quasiparticles</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unified_field_theories">Unified field theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Unified field theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Unified_field_theory" title="Unified field theory">Unified field theory</a></div>
<p>Einstein deemed that all <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_interaction" title="Fundamental interaction">fundamental interactions</a> in nature can be explained in a single theory. Unified field theories were numerous attempts to "merge" several interactions. One of formulations of such theories (as well as field theories in general) is a <i><a href="/wiki/Gauge_theory" title="Gauge theory">gauge theory</a></i>, a generalization of the idea of symmetry. Eventually the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> (see below) succeeded in unification of strong, weak, and electromagnetic interactions. All attempts to unify <a href="/wiki/Gravitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitation">gravitation</a> with something else failed.
</p>
<table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2014</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_Model">Standard Model</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Standard Model">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg/250px-Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg/375px-Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg/500px-Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1390" data-file-height="1330" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The interaction of these particles by <a href="/wiki/Scattering" title="Scattering">scattering</a> and <a href="/wiki/Particle_decay" title="Particle decay">decay</a> provided a key to new fundamental quantum theories. <a href="/wiki/Murray_Gell-Mann" title="Murray Gell-Mann">Murray Gell-Mann</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yuval_Ne%27eman" title="Yuval Ne&#39;eman">Yuval Ne'eman</a> brought some order to these new particles by classifying them according to certain qualities, beginning with what Gell-Mann referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Eightfold_way_(physics)" title="Eightfold way (physics)">Eightfold Way</a>". While its further development, the <a href="/wiki/Quark_model" title="Quark model">quark model</a>, at first seemed inadequate to describe <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong nuclear forces</a>, allowing the temporary rise of competing theories such as the <a href="/wiki/S-Matrix" class="mw-redirect" title="S-Matrix">S-Matrix</a>, the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics" title="Quantum chromodynamics">quantum chromodynamics</a> in the 1970s finalized a set of fundamental and exchange particles, which allowed for the establishment of a "<a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">standard model</a>" based on the mathematics of <a href="/wiki/Gauge_theory" title="Gauge theory">gauge invariance</a>, which successfully described all forces except for <a href="/wiki/Gravitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitation">gravitation</a>, and which remains generally accepted within its domain of application.<sup id="cite_ref-Harvtxt&#124;Kragh&#124;1999_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvtxt|Kragh|1999-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
