</p><p>The Standard Model groups the <a href="/wiki/Electroweak_interaction" title="Electroweak interaction">electroweak interaction</a> theory and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics" title="Quantum chromodynamics">quantum chromodynamics</a> into a structure denoted by the <a href="/wiki/Gauge_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Gauge group">gauge group</a> SU(3)&#215;SU(2)&#215;U(1). The formulation of the unification of the electromagnetic and <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak interactions</a> in the standard model is due to <a href="/wiki/Abdus_Salam" title="Abdus Salam">Abdus Salam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steven_Weinberg" title="Steven Weinberg">Steven Weinberg</a> and, subsequently, <a href="/wiki/Sheldon_Glashow" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheldon Glashow">Sheldon Glashow</a>. Electroweak theory was later confirmed experimentally (by observation of <a href="/wiki/Neutral_current" title="Neutral current">neutral weak currents</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> and distinguished by the 1979 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the 1970s, fundamental particle physics has provided insights into early universe <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> theory proposed as a consequence of Einstein's <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general theory of relativity</a>. However, starting in the 1990s, astronomical observations have also provided new challenges, such as the need for new explanations of galactic stability ("<a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>") and the <a href="/wiki/Accelerating_universe" class="mw-redirect" title="Accelerating universe">apparent acceleration in the expansion of the universe</a> ("<a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>").
</p><p>While accelerators have confirmed most aspects of the Standard Model by detecting expected particle interactions at various collision energies, no theory reconciling general relativity with the Standard Model has yet been found, although <a href="/wiki/Supersymmetry" title="Supersymmetry">supersymmetry</a> and <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a> were believed by many theorists to be a promising avenue forward. The <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">Large Hadron Collider</a>, however, which began operating in 2008, has failed to find any evidence whatsoever that is supportive of supersymmetry and string theory.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmology">Cosmology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Cosmology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">Physical cosmology</a></div>
<p>Cosmology may be said to have become a serious research question with the publication of Einstein's General Theory of Relativity in 1915 although it did not enter the scientific mainstream until the period known as the "<a href="/wiki/Golden_age_of_general_relativity" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden age of general relativity">Golden age of general relativity</a>".
</p><p>About a decade later, in the midst of what was dubbed the "<a href="/wiki/Great_Debate_(astronomy)" title="Great Debate (astronomy)">Great Debate</a>", <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Hubble</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vesto_Slipher" title="Vesto Slipher">Slipher</a> discovered the <a href="/wiki/Expansion_of_universe" class="mw-redirect" title="Expansion of universe">expansion of universe</a> in the 1920s measuring the redshifts of <a href="/wiki/Doppler_spectra" class="mw-redirect" title="Doppler spectra">Doppler spectra</a> from galactic nebulae. Using Einstein's general relativity, <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lemaître">Lemaître</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Gamow" title="George Gamow">Gamow</a> formulated what would become known as the <a href="/wiki/Big_bang_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Big bang theory">big bang theory</a>. A rival, called the <a href="/wiki/Steady_state_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Steady state theory">steady state theory</a> was devised by <a href="/wiki/Fred_Hoyle" title="Fred Hoyle">Hoyle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Gold" title="Thomas Gold">Gold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jayant_Narlikar" title="Jayant Narlikar">Narlikar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Bondi" title="Hermann Bondi">Bondi</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cosmic_background_radiation" title="Cosmic background radiation">Cosmic background radiation</a> was verified in the 1960s by <a href="/wiki/Arno_Allan_Penzias" title="Arno Allan Penzias">Penzias</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Woodrow_Wilson" title="Robert Woodrow Wilson">Wilson</a>, and this discovery favoured the big bang at the expense of the steady state scenario. Later work was by <a href="/wiki/George_Smoot" title="George Smoot">Smoot</a> et al. (1989), among other contributors, using data from the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_Background_explorer" class="mw-redirect" title="Cosmic Background explorer">Cosmic Background explorer</a> (CoBE) and the <a href="/wiki/Wilkinson_Microwave_Anisotropy_Probe" title="Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe">Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe</a> (WMAP) satellites that refined these observations. The 1980s (the same decade of the COBE measurements) also saw the proposal of <a href="/wiki/Inflation_(cosmology)" title="Inflation (cosmology)">inflation theory</a> by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Guth" title="Alan Guth">Guth</a>.
</p><p>Recently the problems of dark matter and dark energy have risen to the top of the cosmology agenda.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Higgs_boson">Higgs boson</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Higgs boson">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CMS_Higgs-event.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/CMS_Higgs-event.jpg/250px-CMS_Higgs-event.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/CMS_Higgs-event.jpg/375px-CMS_Higgs-event.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/CMS_Higgs-event.jpg/500px-CMS_Higgs-event.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1104" data-file-height="1018" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CMS_Higgs-event.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One possible signature of a Higgs boson from a simulated <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a>–proton collision. It decays almost immediately into two jets of <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a> and two <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>, visible as lines.</div></div></div>
<p>On July 4, 2012, physicists working at CERN's <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">Large Hadron Collider</a> announced that they had discovered a new subatomic particle greatly resembling the <a href="/wiki/Higgs_boson" title="Higgs boson">Higgs boson</a>, a potential key to an understanding of why elementary particles have mass and indeed to the existence of diversity and life in the universe.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> For now, some physicists are calling it a "Higgslike" particle.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joe_Incandela" class="mw-redirect" title="Joe Incandela">Joe Incandela</a>, of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Santa_Barbara" title="University of California, Santa Barbara">University of California, Santa Barbara</a>, said, "It's something that may, in the end, be one of the biggest observations of any new phenomena in our field in the last 30 or 40 years, going way back to the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a>, for example."<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Turner_(cosmologist)" title="Michael Turner (cosmologist)">Michael Turner</a>, a cosmologist at the University of Chicago and the chairman of the physics center board, said:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>This is a big moment for particle physics and a crossroads — will this be the high water mark or will it be the first of many discoveries that point us toward solving the really big questions that we have posed?</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Michael_Turner_(cosmologist)" title="Michael Turner (cosmologist)">Michael Turner</a>, University of Chicago<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_72-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Peter_Higgs" title="Peter Higgs">Peter Higgs</a> was one of six physicists, working in three independent groups, who, in 1964, invented the notion of the Higgs field ("cosmic molasses"). The others were <a href="/wiki/Tom_Kibble" title="Tom Kibble">Tom Kibble</a> of <a href="/wiki/Imperial_College_London" title="Imperial College London">Imperial College, London</a>; <a href="/wiki/C._R._Hagen" title="C. R. Hagen">Carl Hagen</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Rochester" title="University of Rochester">University of Rochester</a>; <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Guralnik" title="Gerald Guralnik">Gerald Guralnik</a> of <a href="/wiki/Brown_University" title="Brown University">Brown University</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Englert" title="François Englert">François Englert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Brout" title="Robert Brout">Robert Brout</a>, both of <a href="/wiki/Universit%C3%A9_libre_de_Bruxelles" title="Université libre de Bruxelles">Université libre de Bruxelles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_72-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although they have never been seen, Higgslike fields play an important role in theories of the universe and in string theory. Under certain conditions, according to the strange accounting of Einsteinian physics, they can become suffused with energy that exerts an antigravitational force. Such fields have been proposed as the source of an enormous burst of expansion, known as inflation, early in the universe and, possibly, as the secret of the dark energy that now seems to be speeding up the expansion of the universe.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_72-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_sciences">Physical sciences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Physical sciences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>With increased accessibility to and elaboration upon advanced analytical techniques in the 19th century, physics was defined as much, if not more, by those techniques than by the search for universal principles of motion and energy, and the fundamental nature of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a>.  Fields such as <a href="/wiki/Acoustics" title="Acoustics">acoustics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geophysics" title="Geophysics">geophysics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astrophysics" title="Astrophysics">astrophysics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aerodynamics" title="Aerodynamics">aerodynamics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cryogenics" title="Cryogenics">low-temperature physics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_physics" title="Solid-state physics">solid-state physics</a> joined <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">fluid dynamics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mechanics" title="Mechanics">mechanics</a> as areas of physical research.  In the 20th century, physics also became closely allied with such fields as <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">electrical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aerospace_engineering" title="Aerospace engineering">aerospace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Materials_science" title="Materials science">materials</a> engineering, and physicists began to work in government and industrial laboratories as much as in academic settings. Following World War&#160;II, the population of physicists increased dramatically, and came to be centered on the United States, while, in more recent decades, physics has become a more international pursuit than at any time in its previous history.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_physics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Click the image to see further details.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Rozhanskaya-642-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Rozhanskaya-642_16-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mariam Rozhanskaya and I. S. Levinova (1996), "Statics", p. 642, in <a href="#CITEREFRashedMorelon1996">Rashed &amp; Morelon (1996</a>, pp.&#160;614–642): <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Using a whole body of mathematical methods (not only those inherited from the antique theory of ratios and infinitesimal techniques, but also the methods of the contemporary algebra and fine calculation techniques), Islamic scientists raised statics to a new, higher level. The classical results of Archimedes in the theory of the centre of gravity were generalized and applied to three-dimensional bodies, the theory of ponderable lever was founded and the 'science of gravity' was created and later further developed in medieval Europe. The phenomena of statics were studied by using the dynamic approach so that two trends – statics and dynamics – turned out to be inter-related within a single science, mechanics."<br />"The combination of the dynamic approach with Archimedean hydrostatics gave birth to a direction in science which may be called medieval hydrodynamics."<br />"Archimedean statics formed the basis for creating the fundamentals of the science on specific weight. Numerous fine experimental methods were developed for determining the specific weight, which were based, in particular, on the theory of balances and weighing. The classical works of al-Biruni and al-Khazini can by right be considered as the beginning of the application of experimental methods in medieval science."<br />"Arabic statics was an essential link in the progress of world science. It played an important part in the prehistory of classical mechanics in medieval Europe. Without it classical mechanics proper could probably not have been created.
</p></blockquote></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

Norton &amp; Company. p.&#160;39. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-393-70607-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-393-70607-9"><bdi>978-0-393-70607-9</bdi></a>. <q>Alhazen (or Al-Haytham; 965–1039) was perhaps one of the greatest physicists of all times and a product of the Islamic Golden Age or Islamic Renaissance (7th–13th centuries). He made significant contributions to anatomy, astronomy, engineering, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, medicine, ophthalmology, philosophy, physics, psychology, and visual perception and is primarily attributed as the inventor of the scientific method, for which author Bradley Steffens (2006) describes him as the "first scientist".</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Mind%2C+Brain%2C+and+Education+Science%3A+A+Comprehensive+Guide+to+the+New+Brain-Based+Teaching&amp;rft.pages=39&amp;rft.pub=W.W.+Norton+%26+Company&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-393-70607-9&amp;rft.au=Tracey+Tokuhama-Espinosa&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHistory+of+physics" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
